## [Introduction](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/introduction)9 minutes, 5 links

**Equity compensation** is the practice of granting partial ownership in a company in exchange for work. In its ideal form, equity compensation aligns the interests of individual employees with the goals of the company they work for, which can yield dramatic results in team building, innovation, and longevity of employment. Each of these [contributesÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/history-and-significance) to the creation of valueâ€”for a company, for its users and customers, and for the individuals who work to make it a success.

The [ways equity can be grantedÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/how-equity-is-granted) as compensationâ€”including [restricted stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants), [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), and restricted stock unitsâ€”are **notoriously complex**. Equity compensation involves confounding terminology, legal obscurities, and many high-stakes decisions for those who give and receive it.

If you talk to enough employees and hiring managers, youâ€™ll hear stories of how they or their colleagues met with the painful consequences of not learning enough up front. Though many people learn the basic ideas from personal experience or from colleagues or helpful friends who have been through it before, the intricacies of equity compensation are best understood by tax attorneys, corporate lawyers, and other professionals.

Decisions related to [negotiating an offerÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/offers-and-negotiations) and [exercising stock optionsÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/stock-option-scenarios), in particular, can have **major financial consequences**. Because the value of employee equity is determined by the fate of the company, an employeeâ€™s equity may be [illiquidÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/what-is-private-stock-worth) for a long time or ultimately [worth nothingÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/growth-and-risk), while taxes and the costs of [exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise), if they apply, may not be recouped. Even when a company is doing well, an employee may suffer [catastrophic tax pitfallsÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/tax-dangers) because they didnâ€™t anticipate the tax consequences of their decisions.

Understanding the technicalities of equity compensation does not guarantee that fortune will smile upon you as warmly as it did [the early hiresÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/history-and-significance) of Facebook. But a thorough overview can help you be informed when [discussing with professionalsÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/seeking-professional-advice) for further assistance, make better decisions for your personal situation, and avoid some [commonÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/tax-dangers) and [costlyÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/the-amt-trap) mistakes.

### Why This Guide?

The first edition of this work, written by the same lead authors as the one youâ€™re reading now, received significant feedback and discussion [on Hacker News](https://news.ycombinator.com/item?id=10880726), [on GitHub](https://github.com/jlevy/og-equity-compensation), and from individual experts. Now, Holloway is pleased to publish this new edition of the Guide. Weâ€™ve expanded sections, added resources and visuals, and filled in gaps.

There is [a lot of informationÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/further-reading) about equity compensation [spread](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/spread-and-bargain-element) across blogs and articles that focus on specific components of the topic, such as [vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting), types of [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), or equity levels. We believe there is a need for a consolidated and shared resource, written by and for people on different sides of compensation decisions, including employees, hiring managers, founders, and students. Anyone can feel overwhelmed by the complex details and high-stakes personal choices that this topic involves. This reference exists to answer the needs of beginners and the more experienced.

Holloway and our contributors are motivated by a single purpose: To help readers understand important details and their contexts well enough to make better decisions themselves. The Guide aims to be **practical** (with concrete suggestions and pitfalls to avoid), **thoughtful** (with context and multiple expert perspectives, including divergent opinion on controversial topics), and **concise** (it is dense but contains only notable detailsâ€”still, itâ€™s at least a three-hour read, with links to three hundred sources!).

The Guide does not purport to be either perfect or complete. A reference like this is always in process. Thatâ€™s why weâ€™re currently testing features to enable the Holloway community to suggest improvements, contribute new sections, and call out anything that needs revision. We welcome (and will gladly credit) [your helpÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/please-help).

We especially wish to [recognizeÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/credits) the dozens of people who have helped write, review, edit, and improve it so farâ€”and in the futureâ€”and hope youâ€™ll check back often as it improves.

### Scope

This Guide **currently covers**:

- â–ªEquity compensation in **[C corporations](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/c-corporation-or-c-corp)** in the **United States**.
- â–ªEquity compensation for most employees, advisors, and independent contractors in [private companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies), from startups through larger private [corporations](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/corporation-and-stock-corporation-and-non-stock-corporations).
- â–ªLimited coverage of equity compensation in [public companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies).

Topics **not yet covered**:

- â–ªEquity compensation programs, such as [ESPPs](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/e/espp.asp) in [public companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies). (Weâ€™d like to [see this improveÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/please-help) in the future.)
- â–ªFull details on executive equity compensation.
- â–ªCompensation outside the United States.
- â–ªCompensation in companies other than [C corporations](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/c-corporation-or-c-corp), including [LLCs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limited_liability_company) and [S corporations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/S_corporation), where equity compensation is approached and practiced in very different ways.

For these situations, see [other resourcesÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/when-to-turn-elsewhere) and get [professional adviceÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/seeking-professional-advice).

### Who May Find This Useful

Our aim is to be as helpful to the beginner as to those with more experience. Having talked with employees, CEOs, investors, and lawyers, we can assure you that no matter how much you know about equity compensation, you will likely run into confusion at some point.

If youâ€™re an **employee** or a **candidate for a job**, some of these may apply to you:

- â–ªYouâ€™ve heard phrases like *stock*, *[stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options)*, *[strike price](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/strike-price-or-exercise-price)*, *[ISOs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/incentive-and-non-qualifying-and-statutory-and-non-statutory-stock-options)*, *[RSUs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-units-or-rsus)*, *[83(b) election](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/83-b-election)*, *[409A valuation](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/409a-valuation)*, *[AMT](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/alternative-minimum-tax-or-amt)*, or *[early exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/early-exercise-or-forward-exercise)* and know they are probably important but are mystified by what some of them really mean or whether they apply to your situation.
- â–ªYouâ€™re considering a job offer but donâ€™t know how to [navigate or negotiateÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/offers-and-negotiations) the equity component of the offer.
- â–ªYouâ€™re joining a startup for the first time and are overwhelmed by all the [paperworkÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/documents-and-agreements).
- â–ªYouâ€™re quitting, taking a leave of absence, or are being laid off or fired from a company where you have stock or options and are thinking through the decisions and consequences.
- â–ªA company you work for is going through an [acquisition](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/acquisition-and-sale), [IPO](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/initial-public-offering-or-ipo-and-goes-public), or shutdown.
- â–ªYou have stock in a [private company](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies) and [need cashÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/stock-option-scenarios).

**Founders** or **hiring managers** who need to talk about equity compensation with employees or potential hires will also find this Guide useful. As many entrepreneurs and hiring managers will tell you, this topic isnâ€™t easy on that side of the table, either! Negotiating with candidates and fielding questions from candidates and employees requires understanding the same complex technicalities of equity compensation well.

That said, this topic is **not simple** and we ask that readers be willing to invest time to get through a lot of confusing detail. If youâ€™re in a hurry, or you donâ€™t care to learn the details, this Guide may not be for you. [Seek adviceÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/seeking-professional-advice).

### A Note on Fairness

Much of what you read about equity compensation was written by a single person, from a single vantage point. The authors and editors of this Guide have navigated the territory of equity compensation from the perspective of employees, hiring managers, founders, and lawyers. We do believe that the knowledge here, [combined with professional adviceÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/seeking-professional-advice), can make a significant difference for **both employees and hiring managers**.

One of the difficulties for candidates negotiating equity compensation is that they may have less information about what they are worth than the person hiring them. Companies talk to many candidates and often have access to or pay for expensive market-rate compensation data. While some data on [typical equity levelsÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/typical-employee-equity-levels) have been published online, much of it fails to represent the value of a candidate with their own specific experience in a specific role. However, even without exact data, candidates and hiring managers can develop better mental frameworks to think about [offers and negotiationsÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/offers-and-negotiations).

On the other hand, challenges are not limited to those of employees. Founders and hiring managers also often struggle with talking through the web of technicalities with potential hires, and can make equally poor decisions when making offers. Either over-compensating or under-compensating employees can have unfortunate consequences.

In short, both companies and employees are routinely hurt by uninformed decisions and costly mistakes when it comes to equity compensation. A shared resource is helpful for both sides.

## [Roadmap](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/roadmap)6 minutes, 2 links

### The Holloway Reader

The Holloway Reader youâ€™re using now is designed to help you find and navigate the material you need. **Use the search box.** It will reveal definitions, section-by-section results, and content contained in **the hundreds of resources weâ€™ve linked to** throughout the Guide. Think of it as a mini library of the best content on equity compensation. We also provide mouseover (or short tap on mobile) for **definitions of terms**, related section suggestions, and external links while you read.

### How This Guide Is Organized

This Guide contains a lot of material. And itâ€™s dense. Some readers may wish to read front to back, but you can also **search or navigate directly** to parts that are of interest to you, **referring back** to foundational topics as needed.

Equity compensation lies at the intersection of corporate law, taxation, and employee compensation, and so requires some basic understanding of all three. You might think compensation and taxation are separate topics, but they are so intertwined it would be misleading to explain one without the other. We cover material in logical order, so that if you do read the earlier sections first, later sections on the interactions of tax and compensation will be clearer.

We start with [**Equity Compensation Basics**Â§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/equity-compensation-basics): What compensation and equity are, and why equity is used as compensation.

But before we get much further, we need to talk about what stock is, and how companies are formed. [**Fundamentals of Stock Corporations**Â§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/fundamentals-of-stock-corporations) covers how companies organize their ownership, how stock is issued, [public companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies) and [private companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies), and [IPOs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/initial-public-offering-or-ipo-and-goes-public) and [liquidity](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/liquidity-and-exits-and-liquidity-events-and-liquidations) (which determine when equity is worth cash).

While not everyone reading this works at an early stage company, those who do can benefit from understanding the role of equity in [**Startups and Growth**Â§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/startups-and-growth). This is good context for anyone involved in a [private company](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies) that has taken on [venture capital](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/venture-capital-and-venture-capitalists-or-vcs).

[**How Equity is Granted**Â§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/how-equity-is-granted) is the core of this Guide. We describe the forms in which equity is most commonly granted, including [restricted stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants) grants, [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), and [RSUs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-units-or-rsus).

Now is where it gets messierâ€”taxes:

- â–ª[**Tax Basics**Â§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/tax-basics): A technical summary of how taxation works. Many of the headaches of equity compensation involve how it is taxed, including [ordinary income tax](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/ordinary-income-tax-and-short-term-capital-gains-tax), [long-term capital gains tax](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/long-term-capital-gains-tax), and the lesser-known but sometimes critical [alternative minimum tax](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/alternative-minimum-tax-or-amt).
- â–ª[**Taxes on Equity Compensation**Â§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/taxes-on-equity-compensation): How much tax you owe is greatly affected by the kind of equity you have (such as [restricted stock awards](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants), [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), or RSUs), when you choose to pay (including 83(b) elections), and when you choose to [exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise) options.

After these technical concerns, we move on to how you can think about all this in practice. These sections focus on scenarios common to employees and candidates, but are also of likely interest to founders and hiring managers:

- â–ª[**Plans and Scenarios**Â§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/plans-and-scenarios): Whether you have equity now or will in the future, it is helpful to learn *how to think about* the value of equity and its tax burden. We also cover whether you can [sell private stockÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/can-you-sell-private-stock).
- â–ª[**Offers and Negotiations**Â§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/offers-and-negotiations): Equity often comes up as youâ€™re negotiating or debating whether to accept a job offer. Here we cover what to expect, what to ask, tips and pitfalls, and more.

Finally, we offer some additional resources:

- â–ª[**Documents and Agreements**Â§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/documents-and-agreements): A bit more detail on the actual legal paperwork youâ€™re likely to see as you negotiate and after youâ€™ve accepted an offer.
- â–ª[**Further Reading**Â§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/further-reading): A curated list of what else you can read on the subject, including many papers, books, and articles that have informed this Guide.

### When to Turn Elsewhere

**CEOs**, **CFOs**, **COOs**, or anyone who runs a company or team of significant size should be sure to talk to an equity compensation consultant or a specialist at a law firm to learn about equity compensation plans.

**Founders** looking for an introduction to the legalities of running a company may wish to check out [*Legal Concepts for Founders*](https://handbook.clerky.com/), from Clerky, in addition to talking to a lawyer. Founders should also lean on their investors for advice, as they may have additional experience.

**Executive compensation** at large or [public companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies) is an even more nuanced topic, on both sides of the table. Hire an experienced lawyer or compensation consultant. There are extensive legal [resources](https://www.compensationstandards.com/home/) available on executive compensation.

### Seeking Professional Advice

This Guide does not replace professional advice.

Please read the full [disclaimerÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/disclaimer) and seek professional advice from a lawyer, tax professional, or other compensation expert before making significant decisions.

Does that make reading through these details a waste of time? Not at all. Important decisions rarely should or can be blindly delegated. This Guide *complements but does not replace* the advice you get from professionals. Working with the support of a professional can help you make better decisions when you have an understanding of the topic yourself and know what questions to ask.

## [Equity Compensation Basics](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/equity-compensation-basics)7 minutes, 25 links

### History and Significance

Companies ranging from two-person startups to the [Fortune 500](http://fortune.com/fortune500/) have found that granting partial ownership in a company is among the best methods to attract and retain exceptional talent. In the United States, partial ownership through [stock optionsÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/stock-options) has been a key part of pay for executives and other employees since the 1950s.[*](https://corpgov.law.harvard.edu/2014/10/02/what-has-happened-to-stock-options/) As recently as 2014, **7.2%** of all private sector employees (**8.5 million** people) and **13.1%** of *all* employees of companies with stock held [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), according to the National Center for Employee Ownership.[*](https://www.nceo.org/assets/pdf/articles/GSS-2014-data.pdf) Many believe employee ownership has â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' -0.432899%2c-2.57877 -1.23031%2c-3.61531l-2.76%2c-3.2' data-evernote-id='1533' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3c/g%3e%3c/svg%3e)paidâ€‹[fostered innovations](https://www.wsj.com/articles/reviving-the-flagging-spirit-of-silicon-valley-1428706671) in technology, especially in Silicon Valley, from the [early days](http://www.hp.com/hpinfo/abouthp/histnfacts/publications/measure/pdf/1976_07.pdf) of Hewlett-Packard to recent examples like Facebook. Stock options helped the first 3,000 employees of Facebook enjoy roughly **$23 billion** at the time the company went public.[*](https://www.ft.com/content/d6599ae0-5738-11e1-869b-00144feabdc0)

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-natural-disaster-hurricane' data-evernote-id='170' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cg stroke-linecap='round' fill='none' stroke-linejoin='round' stroke='currentColor' data-evernote-id='1482' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cpath d='M18.8616%2c1.40901c4.10051%2c0.87868 4.10051%2c2.3033 0%2c3.18198c-4.10051%2c0.87868 -10.7487%2c0.87868 -14.8492%2c0c-4.10051%2c-0.87868 -4.10051%2c-2.3033 -1.77636e-15%2c-3.18198c4.10051%2c-0.87868 10.7487%2c-0.87868 14.8492%2c-4.44089e-16' data-evernote-id='1483' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath fill-rule='evenodd' d='M19.405%2c8.225c-1.186%2c0.99 -5.923%2c1.632 -10.709%2c1.4' data-evernote-id='1484' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath fill-rule='evenodd' d='M18.661%2c12.983l6.4818e-07%2c-3.66212e-07c-1.1459%2c0.647414 -2.41838%2c1.03905 -3.73%2c1.148' data-evernote-id='1485' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath fill-rule='evenodd' d='M0.937%2c3c0%2c0 -2.061%2c4.764 7.5%2c10.5c7.5%2c4.5 5.25%2c9.75 5.25%2c9.75c0%2c0 6%2c-2.25 5.25%2c-7.5c-1.473%2c-10.315 3%2c-7.5 3%2c-12.75' data-evernote-id='1486' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3c/g%3e%3c/svg%3e)controversyâ€‹ Some controversy surrounds the use of equity compensation for high-paid executives. [Public companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies) offer executives equity compensation in no small part because of a tax loophole. In 1993, President Bill Clinton attempted to limit executive pay with a new section[*](https://www.treasury.gov/resource-center/tax-policy/tax-analysis/Documents/Firms-Exceeding-162m.pdf) of the Internal Revenue Code. Unfortunately, the legislation backfired; a loophole made performance-based payâ€”including stock optionsâ€”fully tax deductible, thereby creating a dramatic incentive to pay executives through [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options).[*](https://www.epi.org/publication/taxes-executive-compensation/) From 1970â€“79, the average compensation for a CEO of one of the 50 largest firms in the United States was **$1.2M**, of which **11.2%** was from stock options. By 2000â€“05, the same numbers had risen to **$9.2M** and **37%**, respectively.[*](http://www.nber.org/papers/w16585.pdf)

### Growth and Risk

Generally, equity compensation is closely linked to the **growth** of a company. Cash-poor startups persuade early employees to take pay cuts and join their team by offering meaningful [ownerships stakesÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/typical-employee-equity-levels), catering to hopes that the company will one day grow large enough to [go publicÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/ipos) or [be soldÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/sales-and-liquidity) for an ample sum. More mature but still fast-growing companies find offering compensation linked to ownership is more attractive than high cash compensation to many candidates.

With the hope for growth, however, also comes **risk**. Large, fast-growing companies often hit hard times. And [startupsÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/startups-and-growth) routinely fail or yield no returns for investors or workers. According to [a report](http://fortune.com/2017/06/27/startup-advice-data-failure/) by Cambridge Associates and Fortune Magazine, between 1990 and 2010, **about 60%** of [venture capital](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/venture-capital-and-venture-capitalists-or-vcs)-backed companies returned less than the original investment, leaving employees with the painful realization that their startup was not, in fact, the next Google. Of the remaining **40%**, just a select few go on to make a many of their employees wealthy, as has been the case with iconic high-growth companies, like Starbucks,[*](https://www.investopedia.com/articles/markets/120215/if-you-had-invested-right-after-starbucks-ipo.asp) UPS,[*](https://money.cnn.com/1999/11/10/companies/ups/) Amazon,[*](https://techcrunch.com/2017/06/28/a-look-back-at-amazons-1997-ipo/) Google,[*](https://dealbook.nytimes.com/2009/08/19/googles-ipo-5-years-later/) or Facebook.[*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Initial_public_offering_of_Facebook)

### Compensation and Equity

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Compensation** is any remuneration to a person (including employees, contractors, advisors, founders, and board members) for services performed or rendered to a company. Compensation comes in the forms of cash pay (salary and any bonuses) and any non-cash pay, including [benefits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Employee_benefits#United_States) like health insurance, family-related protections, perks, and retirement plans.

Company strategies for compensation are [far from simple](http://firstround.com/review/counterintuitive-comp-tips-for-the-unwary-and-uninitiated/). Beth Scheer, head of talent at the venture fund Homebrew, offers a [thoughtful overview](https://quip.com/HEB3Ah9dYD6o) of compensation in startups.

Another term you may encounter is [*total rewards*](https://www.worldatwork.org/total-rewards-model/), which refers to a model of attracting and retaining employees using a combination of salary and incentive compensation (like equity), benefits, recognition for contribution or commitment (like awards and bonuses), training programs, and initiatives to improve the work environment.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ In the context of compensation and investment, **equity** broadly refers to any kind of ownership in a company that can be held by individuals (like employees or board members) and by other businesses (like [venture capital](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/venture-capital-and-venture-capitalists-or-vcs) firms). One common kind of equity is stock, but equity can take other forms, such as [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) or [warrants](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/warrants), that give ownership rights. Commonly, equity also comes with certain conditions, such as [vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting) or repurchase rights. Note the term *equity* also has [several other](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/e/equity.asp) technical meanings in accounting and real estate.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  [**Equity compensation**](http://www.investopedia.com/terms/e/equity-compensation.asp) is the practice of granting equity in exchange for work.

In this Guide we focus on equity compensation in [stock corporations](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/corporation-and-stock-corporation-and-non-stock-corporations), the kind of company where ownership is represented by stock. (We describe stock in more detail in [the next sectionÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/fundamentals-of-stock-corporations).) Equity compensation in the form of a direct grant of stock with no strings attached is very rare. Instead, employees are given stock with additional restrictions placed on it, or are given contractual rights that later can lead to owning stock. These forms of equity compensation include [restricted stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants), [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), and [restricted stock units](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-units-or-rsus), each of which weâ€™ll [describe in detailÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/how-equity-is-granted).

### The Goals of Equity Compensation

The purpose of equity compensation is threefold:

- â–ª**Attract and retain talent.** When a company already has or can be predicted to have significant financial success, talented people are [incentivized](https://avc.com/2010/10/employee-equity-options/) to work for the company by the prospect of their equity being [worth a lot](https://www.entrepreneur.com/article/253438) of money in the future. The actual probability of life-changing lucre may be low (or at least, lower than you may think if your entire knowledge of startups is watching â€œ[The Social Network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Social_Network)â€). But even a small chance at winning big can be worth the risk to many people, and to some the risk itself can be exciting.
- â–ª**Align incentives.** Even companies that can afford to pay lots of cash may prefer to give employees equity, so that employees work to increase the *future* value of the company. In this way, equity aligns individualsâ€™ incentives with the interests of the company. At its best, this philosophy fosters an environment of teamwork and a â€œrising tides lift all boatsâ€ mentality. It also encourages everyone involved to think [long-term](https://www.cebglobal.com/blogs/equity-compensation-will-stop-short-termism-and-boost-growth/), which is key for company success. As weâ€™ll [discuss laterÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/offers-and-negotiations), the amount of equity youâ€™re offered usually reflects both your contribution to the company and your commitment to the company in the future.
- â–ª**Reduce cash spending.** By giving equity, a company can often pay less in cash compensation to employees now, with the hope of rewarding them later, and put that money toward other investments or operating expenses. This can be essential in the early stages of a company or at other times where there may not be enough revenue to pay large salaries. Equity compensation can also help recruit senior employees or executives who would otherwise command especially high salaries.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

## [Fundamentals of Stock Corporations](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/fundamentals-of-stock-corporations)13 minutes, 35 links

In this section, we describe the basics of how stock and shares are used.

Those familiar with stock, [stock corporations](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/corporation-and-stock-corporation-and-non-stock-corporations), [public companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies), and [private companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies) can [jump aheadÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/how-equity-is-granted) to how those companies grant equity.

### Kinds of Companies

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ A **company** is a legal entity formed under corporate law for the purpose of conducting trade. In the United States, specific rules and regulations govern several kinds of [business entities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Types_of_business_entity#United_States). Federal and state law have significant implications on liability and taxation for each kind of company. Notable types of companies include sole proprietorships, partnerships, limited liability companies (LLCs), S corporations, and [C corporations](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/c-corporation-or-c-corp).

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ A **corporation** is a company that is legally recognized as a single entity. The corporation itself, and not its owners, is obligated to repay debts and accountable under contracts and legal actions (that is, is a â€œ[legal person](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Legal_person)â€). Most commonly, the term *corporation* is used to refer to a **[stock corporation (or joint-stock company)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joint-stock_company)**, which is a corporation where ownership is managed using stock. **Non-stock corporations** that do not issue stock exist as well, the most common being [nonprofit organizations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nonprofit_organization). (A few [less common](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-stock_corporation) for-profit non-stock corporations also exist.)

In practice, people often use the word *company* to mean *corporation*.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Incorporation** is the [legal process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Incorporation_(business)) of forming (or **incorporating**) a new corporation, such as a business or nonprofit. Corporations can be created in any country. In the United States, incorporation is handled by state law, and involves filing [articles of incorporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Articles_of_incorporation) and a variety of other [required information](https://handbook.clerky.com/formation/process) with the Secretary of State. (Note that the formation of companies that are not corporations, such as partnerships or LLCs, is not the same as incorporation.)

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ A [**C corporation (or C corp)**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C_corporation) is a type of stock corporation in the United States with certain federal [tax treatment](https://ct.wolterskluwer.com/resource-center/articles/what-c-corporation). It is the most prevalent kind of corporation.[*](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/c/c-corporation.asp) Most large, well-known American companies are C corporations. C corporations differ from [S corporations](https://www.incorporate.com/starting-a-business/s-corporation) and other business entities in several ways, including how [income](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/income-and-ordinary-income-and-capital-gains) is taxed and who may own stock. C corporations have no limit on the number of [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) allowed to own part of the company. They also allow other corporations, as well as partnerships, trusts, and other businesses, to own stock.

C corps are overwhelmingly popular for early-stage [private companiesÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/public-and-private-companies) looking to sell part of their business in exchange for investment from individuals and organizations like [venture capital](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/venture-capital-and-venture-capitalists-or-vcs) firms (which are often partnerships), and for established [public companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies) selling large numbers of stock to individuals and other companies on the [public exchange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stock_exchange).

In practice, for a few reasons, these companies are usually formed in Delaware, so legalities of all this are defined in Delaware law.[*](https://www.quora.com/Why-do-most-technology-startups-incorporate-in-Delaware)[*](https://www.nytimes.com/2012/07/01/business/how-delaware-thrives-as-a-corporate-tax-haven.html) You can think of Delaware law as the primary â€œlanguageâ€ of U.S. corporate law. Incorporating a company in Delaware has evolved into a national standard for high-growth companies, regardless of where they are physically located.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-diamond' data-evernote-id='171' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cg stroke='currentColor' data-evernote-id='1452' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cpath d='M12%2c16.5h-1.63918e-08c-0.207107%2c9.05293e-09 -0.375%2c0.167893 -0.375%2c0.375c9.05293e-09%2c0.207107 0.167893%2c0.375 0.375%2c0.375c0.207107%2c-9.05293e-09 0.375%2c-0.167893 0.375%2c-0.375v1.63918e-08c0%2c-0.207107 -0.167893%2c-0.375 -0.375%2c-0.375v0' stroke-linecap='round' fill='none' stroke-linejoin='round' data-evernote-id='1453' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath fill='none' stroke-linecap='round' d='M12%2c13.5v-7.5' data-evernote-id='1454' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M1.28%2c13.281l-2.03219e-07%2c-2.03466e-07c-0.706594%2c-0.707452 -0.706594%2c-1.85355 4.06438e-07%2c-2.561l9.44%2c-9.439l1.06752e-07%2c-1.07012e-07c0.70521%2c-0.706924 1.84997%2c-0.708314 2.5569%2c-0.00310355c0.00103584%2c0.00103333 0.00207043%2c0.00206792 0.00310376%2c0.00310376l9.44%2c9.439l9.12739e-08%2c9.13848e-08c0.706594%2c0.707452 0.706594%2c1.85355 -1.82548e-07%2c2.561l-9.44%2c9.439l-2.02792e-08%2c2.02611e-08c-0.707109%2c0.706479 -1.85289%2c0.706479 -2.56%2c-4.05222e-08Z' stroke-linecap='round' fill='none' stroke-linejoin='round' data-evernote-id='1455' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3c/g%3e%3c/svg%3e)cautionâ€‹ This Guide focuses specifically on C corporations and [does not coverÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/scope) how equity compensation works in LLCs, S [corporations](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/corporation-and-stock-corporation-and-non-stock-corporations), partnerships, or sole proprietorships. Both equity and compensation are handled in significantly different ways in each of these kinds of businesses.

Loosely, one way to think about companies is that they are simply a set of [contracts](https://www.law.cornell.edu/wex/contract), negotiated over time between the people who own and operate the company, and which are enforced by the government, that aligns the interests of everyone involved in creating things customers are willing to pay for. Key to these contracts is a way to precisely track ownership of the company; issuing stock is how companies often choose to do this.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

### Stock and Shares

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  [**Stock**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stock) is a legal invention that represents ownership in a company. [**Shares**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Share_(finance)) are portions of stock that allow a company to grant ownership to a variety of people or other companies in flexible ways. Each **shareholder (or stockholder)**, as these owners are called, holds a specific number of shares. Founders, investors, employees, [board members](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/board-of-directors-and-inside-directors-and-outside-directors-and-board-members-and-board-seat), contractors, advisors, and other companies, like law firms, can all be shareholders.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ Stock ownership is often formalized on **stock certificates**, which are fancy pieces of paper that prove who owns the stock.

Sometimes you have stock but donâ€™t have the physical certificate, as it may be held for you at a law office.

Some companies now manage their ownership through online services called *ownership management platforms*, such as [Carta](https://carta.com/). If the company you work for uses an ownership management platform, you will be able to view your stock certificates and stock values online.

Younger companies may also choose to keep their stock *uncertificated*, which means your sole evidence of ownership is your contracts with the company, and your spot on the companyâ€™s [cap table](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/capitalization-table-or-cap-table), without having a separate certificate for it.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  [**Outstanding shares**](http://www.investopedia.com/terms/o/outstandingshares.asp) refer to the total number of shares held by all [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder). This number starts at an essentially arbitrary value (such as 10 million) when the company is created, and thereafter will increase as new shares are added (issued) and granted to people in exchange for money or services.

Outstanding shares may increase or decrease for other reasons too, such as [stock splits and share buybacks](http://www.investorguide.com/article/11713/splits-and-buybacks-explained/), which we wonâ€™t get into here.

Later, we discuss several [subtletiesÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/counting-shares) in how shares are counted.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ Any [shareholder](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) has a **percentage ownership** in the company, determined by dividing the number of shares they own by the number of [outstanding shares](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/outstanding-shares). Although stock paperwork will always list numbers of shares, if share value is uncertain, percentage ownership is often a more meaningful number, particularly if you know or can estimate a likely [valuation](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/valuation) of the company. Even if the number of shares a person has is fixed, their percentage ownership will change over time as the outstanding shares change. Typically, this number is presented in percent or [**basis points**](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/b/basispoint.asp) (hundredths of a percent).

### Public and Private Companies

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  [**Public companies**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_company) are [corporations](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/corporation-and-stock-corporation-and-non-stock-corporations) in which any member of the public can own stock. People can buy and sell the stock for cash on public [stock exchanges](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/e/exchange.asp). The value of a companyâ€™s shares is the value displayed in the stock market reports, so [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) know how much their stock is worth.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ Most smaller companies, including all startups, are [**private companies**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Privately_held_company) with owners who control how those companies operate. Unlike a public company, where anyone is able to buy and sell stock, owners of a private company control who is able to buy and sell stock. There may be few or no transactions, or they may not be publicly known.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

### Governance

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ A [corporation](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/corporation-and-stock-corporation-and-non-stock-corporations) has a **board of directors**, a [group of people](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Board_of_directors) whose legal obligation is to oversee the company and ensure it serves the best interests of the [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder). [Public companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies) are legally obligated to have a board of directors, while private companies often elect to have one. The board typically consists of **inside directors**, such as the CEO, one or two founders, or executives employed by the company, and **outside directors**, who are not involved in the day-to-day workings of the company. These **board members** are elected individuals who have legal, corporate governance rights and duties when it comes to voting on key company decisions. A board member is said to have a **board seat** at the company.

Boards of directors range from 3 to 31 members, with an average size of 9.[*](http://www.investopedia.com/articles/analyst/03/111903.asp) Boards are almost always an odd number in order to avoid tie votes. Itâ€™s worth noting that the state of California requires [public companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies) to have at least one woman on their boards.[*](https://www.nytimes.com/2018/09/30/business/women-corporate-boards-california.html)

Key decisions of the board are made formally in *board meetings* or in writing (called *written consent*).[*](https://www.dlapiperaccelerate.com/knowledge/2017/board-action-meetings-vs-written-consents.html) Many decisions around granting equity to employees are approved by the board of directors.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

### IPOs

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ A [private company](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies) becomes a [public company](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies) in a process called an [**initial public offering (IPO)**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Initial_public_offering). Historically, only private companies with a strong track record of years of growth have considered themselves ready to take this significant step. The IPO has [pros and cons](https://www.investopedia.com/university/ipo/ipo.asp) that include exchanging a host of high regulatory costs for the benefits of significant capital. After a company â€œIPOsâ€ or â€œ**goes public**," investors and the general public can buy stock, and existing [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) can sell their stock far more easily than when the company was private.

Companies take years to IPO after being formed. The median time between a companyâ€™s founding and its IPO has been increasing. According to a Harvard report, companies that went public in **2016** took **7.7 years** to do so, compared to **3.1 years** for companies that went public in **1996**.[*](https://corpgov.law.harvard.edu/2017/05/25/2017-ipo-report/)

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

### Sales and Liquidity

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-triangle' data-evernote-id='172' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cg stroke='currentColor' data-evernote-id='1460' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cpath d='M12%2c18.75h-1.63918e-08c-0.207107%2c9.05293e-09 -0.375%2c0.167893 -0.375%2c0.375c9.05293e-09%2c0.207107 0.167893%2c0.375 0.375%2c0.375c0.207107%2c-9.05293e-09 0.375%2c-0.167893 0.375%2c-0.375v1.63918e-08c0%2c-0.207107 -0.167893%2c-0.375 -0.375%2c-0.375v0' stroke-linecap='round' fill='none' stroke-linejoin='round' data-evernote-id='1461' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath fill='none' stroke-linecap='round' d='M12%2c15.75v-7.5' data-evernote-id='1462' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M13.621%2c1.76l3.9326e-08%2c8.00607e-08c-0.43975%2c-0.895254 -1.52198%2c-1.26451 -2.41724%2c-0.824762c-0.358575%2c0.176133 -0.648629%2c0.466187 -0.824762%2c0.824762l-9.473%2c19.298l-1.5871e-08%2c3.22857e-08c-0.370583%2c0.753864 -0.0598732%2c1.66541 0.69399%2c2.03599c0.209097%2c0.102788 0.439014%2c0.156164 0.67201%2c0.156009h19.456l4.34975e-08%2c2.88658e-11c0.840025%2c0.000557409 1.52145%2c-0.679965 1.52201%2c-1.51999c0.000154607%2c-0.232996 -0.0532212%2c-0.462912 -0.156009%2c-0.67201Z' stroke-linecap='round' fill='none' stroke-linejoin='round' data-evernote-id='1463' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3c/g%3e%3c/svg%3e)dangerâ€‹ With [private companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies), it can be very [hard to knowÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/what-is-private-stock-worth) the value of equity. Because the value of private company stock is not determined by regular trades on public markets, [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) can only make educated guesses about the likely future value, at a time when they will be able to sell stock.

After all, [private company](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies) stock is simply a legal agreement that entitles you to something of highly uncertain value, and could well be worthless in the future, or highly valuable, depending on the fate of the company.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='173' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cg stroke-linecap='round' fill='none' stroke-linejoin='round' stroke='currentColor' data-evernote-id='1488' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cpath d='M8.25%2c23.261l-5.223%2c-5.224l2.11416e-08%2c2.18395e-08c-1.01872%2c-1.05235 -0.991456%2c-2.73128 0.0608933%2c-3.75c1.02827%2c-0.995404 2.66084%2c-0.995404 3.68911%2c-4.22832e-08l1.473%2c1.474v-12l3.37508e-14%2c4.52987e-07c-2.50178e-07%2c-1.65685 1.34315%2c-3 3%2c-3c1.65685%2c-2.50178e-07 3%2c1.34315 3%2c3c0%2c0 0%2c1.77636e-15 0%2c1.77636e-15v7.5h1.5l-2.62268e-07%2c5.32907e-15c3.31371%2c-1.44847e-07 6%2c2.68629 6%2c6v6' data-evernote-id='1489' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M14.25%2c9.76h-6' data-evernote-id='1490' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M14.25%2c5.26h-6' data-evernote-id='1491' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M3.75%2c5.261l4.5%2c2.25l-4.5%2c2.25' data-evernote-id='1492' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3c/g%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ Weâ€™ll discuss the notion of a company officially assigning a [fair market valueÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/409a-valuations) later, but even if a company gives you a value for your stock for tax and accounting purposes, it doesnâ€™t mean you can expect to sell it for that value!

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ An [**acquisition**](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/a/acquisition.asp) is the purchase of more than 50% of the shares of one company (the acquired company) by another company (the purchaser). This is also called a **sale** of the acquired company. In an acquisition, the acquired company cedes control to the purchaser.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ The ability to buy and sell stock is called **liquidity**. In startups and many [private companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies), it is often hard to sell stock until the company is sold or [goes public](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/initial-public-offering-or-ipo-and-goes-public), so there is little or no liquidity for [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) until those events occur. Thus, sales and [IPOs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/initial-public-offering-or-ipo-and-goes-public) are called both **exits** and **liquidity events**. Sales, dissolutions, and bankruptcy are all called **liquidations**.

Often people wish they could sell stock in a [private company](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies), because they would prefer having the cash. This is only possible occasionally. We get into the details [laterÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/can-you-sell-private-stock), in our section on selling private stock.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ A [**dividend**](http://www.investopedia.com/terms/d/dividend.asp) is a distribution of a companyâ€™s profit to [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder), authorized by the [board of directors](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/board-of-directors-and-inside-directors-and-outside-directors-and-board-members-and-board-seat). Established [public companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies) and some [private companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies) pay dividends, but this is rare among startups and companies focused on rapid growth, since they often wish to re-invest their profits into expanding the business, rather than paying that money back to shareholders. Amazon, for example, has [never paid](https://www.fool.com/investing/2017/12/28/will-amazon-start-paying-a-dividend-in-2018.aspx) dividends.

## [Startups and Growth](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/startups-and-growth)16 minutes, 43 links

If youâ€™re considering working for a startup, what we cover next on how these early-stage companies raise money and grow is helpful in understanding what your equity may be worth.

If youâ€™re only concerned with large and established companies, you can skip ahead to [how equity is grantedÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/how-equity-is-granted).

### Startups

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ A [**startup**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Startup_company) is an emerging company, typically a [private company](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies), that aspires to grow quickly in [size, revenue, and influence](http://www.paulgraham.com/growth.html). Once a company is established in the market and successful for a while, it usually stops being called a startup.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='174' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ Unlike the terminology around [corporations](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/corporation-and-stock-corporation-and-non-stock-corporations), which has legal significance, the term *startup* is informal, and not everyone uses it consistently.

Startups are not the same as small businesses. Small businesses, like a coffee shop or plumbing business, typically intend to grow slowly and organically, while relying much less on investment capital and equity compensation. Distinguished startup investor [Paul Graham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Graham_(programmer)) has [emphasized](http://www.paulgraham.com/growth.html) that itâ€™s best to think of a startup as any [early stageÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/stages-of-a-startup) company intending to grow quickly.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-sigma' data-evernote-id='175' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cpath stroke-linecap='round' fill='none' stroke-linejoin='round' stroke='currentColor' d='M20.75%2c0.748h-16.939l-3.92561e-08%2c-1.78026e-11c-0.414214%2c-0.000187816 -0.750152%2c0.335446 -0.75034%2c0.74966c-9.01727e-05%2c0.198869 0.0788063%2c0.389631 0.21934%2c0.53034l8.909%2c8.909l-4.5912e-08%2c-4.59259e-08c0.585573%2c0.58575 0.585573%2c1.53525 9.1824e-08%2c2.121l-8.909%2c8.909l6.69093e-09%2c-6.71707e-09c-0.292711%2c0.293855 -0.291784%2c0.769361 0.00207082%2c1.06207c0.140492%2c0.139945 0.33063%2c0.218645 0.528929%2c0.218928h16.939' data-evernote-id='1511' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3c/svg%3e)technicalâ€‹  [C corporations](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/c-corporation-or-c-corp) dominate the startup ecosystem. LLCs tend to be better suited for slower-growth companies that intend to distribute profits instead of re-investing them for growth. Because of this, and for complex reasons related to how their capital is raised, [venture capitalists](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/venture-capital-and-venture-capitalists-or-vcs) significantly prefer to invest in C corporations.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

### Fundraising, Growth, and Dilution

Many large and successful companies began as startups. In general, startups rely on investors to help fund rapid growth.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Fundraising** is the process of seeking capital to build or scale a business. Selling shares in a business to investors is one form of fundraising, as are loans and [initial coin offerings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Initial_coin_offering). **Financing** refers both to fundraising from outside sources and to bringing in revenue from selling a product or service.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Venture capital** is a form of financing for early-stage companies that individual investors or investment firms provide in exchange for partial ownership, or equity, in a company. These investors are called **venture capitalists (or VCs)**. Venture capitalists invest in companies they perceive to be capable of growing quickly and commanding significant market share. â€œVentureâ€ refers to the risky nature of investing in early-stage businessesâ€”typically startupsâ€”with unproven business models.

A startup goes through several [stages of growthÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/stages-of-a-startup) as it raises capital based on the hope and expectation that the company will grow and make more money in the future.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ Companies add (or â€œissueâ€) shares during fundraising, which can be exchanged for cash from investors. As the number of [outstanding shares](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/outstanding-shares) goes up, the [percentage ownership](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/percentage-ownership-and-basis-points) of each [shareholder](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) goes down. This is called [**dilution**](http://www.investopedia.com/terms/d/dilution.asp).

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='176' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ Dilution doesnâ€™t necessarily mean that youâ€™re losing anything as a [shareholder](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder). As a company issues stock and raises money, the smaller percentage of the company you *do* have could be worth more. The size of your slice gets relatively smaller, but, if the company is growing, the size of the cake gets bigger. For example, a typical startup might have three rounds of funding, with each round of funding issuing 20% more shares. At the end of the three rounds, there are more outstanding sharesâ€”roughly 73% more in this case, since 120%Ã—120%Ã—120% is 173%â€”and each shareholder owns proportionally less of the company.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ The [**valuation**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valuation_(finance)) of the company is the present value investors believe the company has. If the company is doing well, growing revenue or showing indications of future revenue (like a growing number of users or traction in a promising market), the companyâ€™s valuation will usually be on the rise. That is, the [price](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Share_price) for an investor to buy one share of the company would be increasing.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-triangle' data-evernote-id='177' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)dangerâ€‹ Of course, things do not always go well, and the valuation of a company does not always go up. It can happen that a company fails entirely and all ownership stakes become worthless, or that the valuation is lower than expected and [certain kindsÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/classes-of-stock) of shares become worthless while other kinds have some value. When investors and leadership in a company expect the company to do better than it actually does, it can have a lot of disappointing consequences for [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder).

### Dilution Illustrations

These visualizations illustrate how ownership of a venture-backed company evolves as funding is raised. One scenario imagines changes to ownership in a well-performing startup, and the other is loosely based on a careful analysis of Zipcar,[*](http://reactionwheel.net/2018/05/zipcar-fundraising-breakdown.html) a ride-sharing company that experienced substantial [dilution](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/dilution) before eventually going public and being [acquiredÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/sales-and-liquidity). These diagrams simplify complexities such as the ones discussed in that analysis, but they give a sense of how ownership can be diluted.

â€‹

â€‹

### Stages of a Startup

Understanding the value of stock and equity in a startup requires a grasp of the stages of growth a startup goes through. These stages are largely reflected in how much funding has been raisedâ€”how much ownership, in the form of shares, has been sold for capital.

Very roughly, [typical stages](http://blog.eladgil.com/2011/03/how-funding-rounds-differ-seed-series.html) are:

- â–ª[**Bootstrapped**](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/b/bootstrapping.asp) (little funding or self-funded): Founders are figuring out what to build, or theyâ€™re starting to build with their own time and resources.
- â–ª[**Series Seed**](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/s/seedcapital.asp) (roughly $250K to $2 million in funding): Figuring out the product and market. The low end of this spectrum is now often called **pre-seed**.
- â–ª[**Series A**](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/s/seriesa.asp) ($2 to $15 million): Scaling the product and making the business model work.
- â–ª[**Series B**](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/s/series-b-financing.asp) (tens of millions): Scaling the business.
- â–ª**Series C, D, E, et cetera** (tens to hundreds of millions): Continued scaling of the business.

Keep in mind that these numbers are more typical for startups located in California. The amount raised at various stages is typically smaller for companies located outside of Silicon Valley, where what would be called a seed round may be called a Series A in, say, Houston, Denver, or Columbus, where there are fewer companies competing for investment from fewer venture firms, and costs associated with growth (including providing livable salaries) are lower.[*](https://www.nytimes.com/2016/08/22/business/economy/bay-area-start-ups-find-low-cost-outposts-in-arizona.html)[*](http://www.chicagotribune.com/bluesky/technology/ct-silicon-valley-midwest-startups-20150925-story.html)

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-diamond' data-evernote-id='178' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)cautionâ€‹ Most startups donâ€™t get far. According to an analysis of [angel investments](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/a/angelinvestor.asp), by Susa Ventures general partner [Leo Polovets](http://codingvc.com/), **more than half** of investments fail; **one in 3** are small successes (1X to 5X returns); **one in 8** are big successes (5X to 30X); and **one in 20** are huge successes (30X+).[*](http://codingvc.com/valuing-employee-options/)

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-diamond' data-evernote-id='179' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)cautionâ€‹ Each stage reflects the reduction of risk and increased [dilution](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/dilution). For this reason, the amount of equity team members get is higher in the earlier stages (starting with founders) and increasingly lower as a company matures. (See the picture above.)

### The Option Pool

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ At some point early on, generally before the first employees are hired, a number of shares will be reserved for an employee **[option pool](http://www.investopedia.com/terms/o/option-pool.asp) (or employee pool)**. The option pool is part of a legal structure called an equity incentive plan. A typical size for the option pool is 20% of the stock of the company, but, especially for earlier stage companies, the option pool can be 10%, 15%, or other sizes.

Once the pool is established, the companyâ€™s [board of directors](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/board-of-directors-and-inside-directors-and-outside-directors-and-board-members-and-board-seat) grants stock from the pool to employees as they join the company.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-sigma' data-evernote-id='180' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)technicalâ€‹ Well-advised companies will reserve in the option pool only what they [expect to use](http://siliconhillslawyer.com/2014/05/01/option-pool-not-ocean-startups/) over the next 12 months or so; otherwise, given how equity grants are usually promised, they may be over-granting equity. The whole pool may never be fully used, but companies should still try not to reserve more than they plan to use. The size of the pool is determined by [complex factors](http://venturehacks.com/articles/option-pool-shuffle) between founders and investors. Itâ€™s worth employees (and [founders](https://www.cooleygo.com/negotiating-option-pool/)) understanding that a small pool can be a good thing in that it reflects the company preserving ownership in negotiations with investors. The size of the pool may be increased later.

### Counting Shares

There are some key subtleties youâ€™re likely to come across in the way [outstanding sharesÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/stock-and-shares) are counted:

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  [Private companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies) always have what are referred to as **authorized but unissued** shares, referring to shares that are authorized in legal paperwork but have not actually been issued. Until they are issued, the [unissued stock](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/u/unissuedstock.asp) these shares represent doesnâ€™t mean anything to the company or to [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder): no one owns it.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='181' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ For example, a [corporation](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/corporation-and-stock-corporation-and-non-stock-corporations) might have 100 million *authorized* shares, but will only have *issued* 10 million shares. In this example, the corporation would have 90 million *authorized but unissued* shares. When you are trying to determine what percentage a number of shares represents, you do *not* make reference to the authorized but unissued shares.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='182' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ You actually want to know the total issued shares, but even this number can be confusing, as it can be computed [more than one way](http://www.mattbartus.com/option-grants-fully-diluted-or-issued-and-outstanding/). Typically, people count shares in two ways: *issued and outstanding* and *[fully diluted](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/fully-diluted)*.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Issued and outstanding** refers to the number of shares actually issued by a company to [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder), and does not include shares that others may have an option to purchase.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Fully diluted** refers to all of the shares that a company has issued, all of the shares that have been set aside in a stock incentive plan, and all of the shares that could be issued if all convertible securities (such as outstanding warrants) were exercised.

A key difference between fully diluted shares and shares issued and outstanding is that the total of fully diluted shares will include all the shares in the employee [option pool](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/option-pool-or-employee-pool) that are reserved but *not yet issued to* employees.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='plain-diamond-outline' data-evernote-id='183' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cpath stroke-linecap='round' fill='none' stroke-linejoin='round' stroke='currentColor' d='M1.28%2c13.281l-2.03219e-07%2c-2.03466e-07c-0.706594%2c-0.707452 -0.706594%2c-1.85355 4.06438e-07%2c-2.561l9.44%2c-9.439l1.06752e-07%2c-1.07012e-07c0.70521%2c-0.706924 1.84997%2c-0.708314 2.5569%2c-0.00310355c0.00103584%2c0.00103333 0.00207043%2c0.00206792 0.00310376%2c0.00310376l9.44%2c9.439l9.12739e-08%2c9.13848e-08c0.706594%2c0.707452 0.706594%2c1.85355 -1.82548e-07%2c2.561l-9.44%2c9.439l-2.02792e-08%2c2.02611e-08c-0.707109%2c0.706479 -1.85289%2c0.706479 -2.56%2c-4.05222e-08Z' data-evernote-id='1715' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3c/svg%3e)importantâ€‹ If youâ€™re trying to figure out the likely percentage a number of shares will be worth in the future, itâ€™s best to know the number of shares that are fully diluted.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-sigma' data-evernote-id='184' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)technicalâ€‹ Even the fully diluted number may not take into account outstanding convertible securities (like convertible notes) that are *waiting* to be converted into stock at a future milestone. For a more complete understanding, in addition to asking about the fully-diluted capitalization you can ask about any convertible securities outstanding that are not included in that number.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='185' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ The terminology mentioned here isnâ€™t universally applied. Itâ€™s worth discussing these terms with your company to be sure youâ€™re on the same page.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ A **capitalization table (cap table)** is a [table](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/c/capitalization-table.asp) (often a spreadsheet or other official record) that records the ownership stakes, including number and class of shares, of all [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) in the company. It is updated as stock is granted to new shareholders.[*](https://www.cooleygo.com/what-is-a-cap-table/)

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

### Classes of Stock

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ Investors often ask for rights to be paid back first in exchange for their investment. The way these different rights are handled is by creating different [**classes of stock**](https://www.upcounsel.com/classes-of-stock). (These are also sometimes called [**classes of shares**](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/c/class.asp), though that term has another meaning in the context of mutual funds.)

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ Two important classes of stock are [**common stock**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_stock) and [**preferred stock**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Preferred_stock). In general, preferred stock has â€œ[rights, preferences, and privileges](https://www.americanbar.org/groups/business_law/publications/blt/2014/01/04_bigler/)â€ that common stock does not have. Typically, investors get preferred stock, and founders and employees get common stock (or stock options).

The exact number of classes of stock and the differences between them can vary company to company, and, in a startup, these can vary at [each roundÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/stages-of-a-startup) of funding.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='186' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ Another term youâ€™re likely to hear is [*foundersâ€™ stock*](http://www.alleywatch.com/2013/08/what-is-founders-stock-legally/), which is (usually) common stock allocated at a companyâ€™s formation, but otherwise doesnâ€™t have any different rights from other common stock.[*](https://lsvp.wordpress.com/2008/09/15/what-entrepreneurs-need-to-know-about-founders-stock/)

Although preferred stock rights are too complex to cover fully, we can give a few key details:

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  [Preferred stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/common-stock-and-preferred-stock) usually has a [**liquidation preference (or preference)**](http://www.investopedia.com/terms/l/liquidation-preference.asp), meaning the preferred stock owners will be paid before the [common stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/common-stock-and-preferred-stock) owners when a [liquidity eventÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/sales-and-liquidity) occurs, such as if the company is sold or [goes public](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/initial-public-offering-or-ipo-and-goes-public).

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ A company is in [**liquidation overhang**](https://equityzen.com/blog/startup-valuations-and-liquidation-preference-overhang/) when the value of the company doesnâ€™t reach the dollar amount investors put into it. Because of liquidation preference, those holding [preferred stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/common-stock-and-preferred-stock) (investors) will have to be paid before those holding [common stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/common-stock-and-preferred-stock) (employees). If investors have put millions of dollars into a company and itâ€™s sold, employeesâ€™ equity wonâ€™t be worth anything if the company is in [liquidation](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/liquidity-and-exits-and-liquidity-events-and-liquidations) overhang and the sale doesnâ€™t exceed that amount.[*](https://avc.com/2010/10/employee-equity-the-liquidation-overhang/)

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='187' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ The complexities of the liquidation preference are [infamous](https://venturebeat.com/2010/08/16/beware-the-trappings-of-liquidation-preference/). Itâ€™s worth understanding that investors and entrepreneurs negotiate a lot of the details around preferences, including:

- â–ª

The *multiple*, a number designating how many times the investor must be paid back before common [shareholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) receive proceeds. (Often the multiple is 1X, but it can be 2X or higher.)

- â–ª

Whether [preferred stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/common-stock-and-preferred-stock) is [*participating*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Participating_preferred_stock), meaning investors get their money back and also participate in proceeds from [common stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/common-stock-and-preferred-stock).

- â–ª

Whether there is a *cap*, which limits the payout if it is participating.

- â–ª

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-sigma' data-evernote-id='188' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)technicalâ€‹  â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-video-game-key' data-evernote-id='189' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cg stroke-linecap='round' fill='none'
