-2.9%2c-8.81382c-3.23468%2c-1.63306 -7.18076%2c-0.334683 -8.81382%2c2.9c-0.938786%2c1.8595 -0.938786%2c4.05432 -3.4747e-07%2c5.91382Z' data-evernote-id='1525' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3c/g%3e%3c/svg%3e)loginâ€‹[This primer](https://medium.com/@CharlesYu/the-ultimate-guide-to-liquidation-preferences-478dda9f9332) by Charles Yu gives a concise overview. Founders and companies are affected significantly and in subtle ways by these considerations. For example, as lawyer JosÃ© Ancer points out, common and preferred [stockholders](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) are typically quite different and their incentives [sometimes](http://siliconhillslawyer.com/2017/10/13/common-stock-v-preferred-stock/)  [diverge](http://siliconhillslawyer.com/2018/02/07/board-works-common-stock/).

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='plain-diamond-outline' data-evernote-id='190' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)importantâ€‹ For the **purposes of an employee who holds [common stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/common-stock-and-preferred-stock)**, the most important thing to understand about [preferences](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/liquidation-preference-or-preference) is that theyâ€™re not likely to matter if a company does well in the long term. In that case, every [stockholder](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) has valuable stock they can eventually sell. But if a company fails or [exitsÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/sales-and-liquidity) for less than investors had hoped, the preferred stockholders are generally first in line to be paid back. Depending on how favorable the terms are for the investor, if the company [exits](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/liquidity-and-exits-and-liquidity-events-and-liquidations) at a low or modest [valuation](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/valuation), itâ€™s likely that common shareholders will receive littleâ€”or nothing at all.

## [How Equity Is Granted](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/how-equity-is-granted)23 minutes, 35 links

In this section weâ€™ll lay out how equity is granted in practice, including the differences, benefits, and drawbacks of common types of equity compensation, including [restricted stock awards](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants), [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), and [restricted stock units](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-units-or-rsus) (RSUs). Weâ€™ll go over a few less common types as well. While the [intentÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/the-goals-of-equity-compensation) of each kind of equity grant is similar, they differ in many ways, particularly around how they are [taxedÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/taxes-on-equity-compensation).

Except in rare cases where it may be negotiable, the type of equity you get is up to the company you work for. In general, larger companies grant [RSUs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-units-or-rsus), and startups grant [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), and occasionally executives and very early employees get [restricted stock awards](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants).

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

### Restricted Stock Awards

At face value, the most direct approach to equity compensation would be for the company to award stock to an employee in exchange for work. In practice, it turns out a company will only want to do this with restrictions on how and when the stock is fully owned.

Even so, this is actually one of the least common ways to get equity. We mention it first because it is the simplest form of equity compensation, useful for comparison as things get more complex.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ A **restricted stock award** is when a company grants someone stock as a form of compensation. The stock awarded has additional conditions on it, including a [vesting schedule](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting-schedule), so is called **restricted stock**. Restricted stock awards may also be called simply **stock awards** or **stock grants**.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-sigma' data-evernote-id='191' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)technicalâ€‹ What *restricted* means here is actually [complex](https://github.com/jlevy/og-equity-compensation/issues/24). It refers to the fact that the stock (i) has certain restrictions on it (like transfer restrictions) required for [private company](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies) stock, and (ii) will be subject to repurchase at cost pursuant to a [vesting schedule](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting-schedule). The repurchase right lapses over the service-based [vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting) period, which is what is meant in this case by the stock â€œvesting.â€

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='192' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ Restricted stock awards are [not the same](https://www.fool.com/knowledge-center/the-difference-between-a-restricted-stock-unit-res.aspx) thing as [restricted stock units](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-units-or-rsus).

Typically, stock awards are limited to executives or very early hires, since once the value of the shares increases, the tax burden of receiving them (without paying the company for their value) can be too great for most people. Usually, instead of restricted stock, an employee will get [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options).

### Stock Options

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  [**Stock options**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Employee_stock_option) are contracts that allow individuals to buy a specified number of shares in the company they work for at a fixed price. Stock options are the most common way early-stage companies grant equity.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ A person who has received a stock option grant is not a [shareholder](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) until they **exercise** their option, which means purchasing some or all of their shares at the strike price. Prior to exercising, an option holder does not have voting rights.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ The **strike price (or exercise price)** is the fixed price per share at which stock can be purchased, as set in a stock option agreement. The strike price is generally set lower (often much lower) than what people expect will be the *future* value of the stock, which means selling the stock down the road could be profitable.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='193' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹  *Stock options* is a confusing term. In investment, an *option* is a right (but not an obligation) to buy something at a certain price within a certain time frame. Youâ€™ll often see stock options discussed in the context of [investment](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/s/stockoption.asp). What investors in financial markets call *stock options* are indeed options on stock, but they are not *compensatory* stock options awarded for services. In this Guide, and most likely in any conversation you have with an employer, anyone who says â€œstock optionsâ€ will be referring to compensatory stock options.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='194' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹  [Stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) are not the same as stock; they are only the *right to buy stock* at a certain price and under a set of conditions specified in an employeeâ€™s stock option agreement. Weâ€™ll get into these conditions next.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-sigma' data-evernote-id='195' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)technicalâ€‹ Although everyone typically refers to â€œstock optionsâ€ in the plural, when you receive a [stock option](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) grant, you are receiving *an option* to purchase a given number of shares. So technically, itâ€™s incorrect to say someone â€œhas 10,000 stock options.â€

Itâ€™s best to understand the financial and [tax implicationsÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/taxes-on-isos-and-nsos) before deciding [when to exerciseÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/stock-option-scenarios) options. In order for the option to be tax-free to receive, the [strike price](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/strike-price-or-exercise-price) must be the [fair market value](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/fair-market-value-or-fmv) of the stock on the date the option is granted.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-sigma' data-evernote-id='196' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)technicalâ€‹ Those familiar with [stock trading](https://www.investopedia.com/university/stocks/stocks3.asp) (or those with economics degrees) will tell you about the [**Black-Scholes model**](https://www.investopedia.com/university/options-pricing/black-scholes-model.asp), a general mathematical model for determining the value of options. While theoretically sound, this does not have as much practical application in the context of employee [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options).

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

### Vesting and Cliffs

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Vesting** is the process of gaining full legal rights to something. In the context of compensation, founders, executives, and employees typically gain rights to their grant of equity incrementally over time, subject to restrictions. People may refer to their shares or [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) vesting, or may say that a person is vesting or has fully vested.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ In the majority of cases, vesting occurs incrementally over time, according to a **vesting schedule**. A person vests only while they work for the company. If the person quits or is terminated immediately, they get no equity, and if they stay for years, theyâ€™ll get most or all of it.

Awards of stock, [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), and [RSUs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-units-or-rsus) are almost always subject to a vesting schedule.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ Vesting schedules can have a **cliff** designating a length of time that a person must work before they vest at all.

For example, if your equity award had a one-year cliff and you only worked for the company for 11 months, you would not get anything, since you havenâ€™t vested in any part of your award. Similarly, if the company is sold within a year of your arrival, depending on what your paperwork says, you may receive nothing on the sale of the company.

A very common [vesting schedule](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting-schedule) is [vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting) over **4 years**, with a **1 year** cliff. This means you get 0% vesting for the first 12 months, 25% vesting at the 12th month, and 1/48th (2.08%) more vesting each month until the 48th month. If you leave just before a year is up, you get nothing, but if you leave after 3 years, you get 75%.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ In some cases, [vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting) may be triggered by specific events outside of the [vesting schedule](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting-schedule), according to contractual terms called **accelerated vesting (or acceleration)**. Two kinds of accelerated vesting that are commonly negotiated are if the company is sold or undergoes a merger (**single trigger**) or if itâ€™s sold and the person is fired (**double trigger**).

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-natural-disaster-hurricane' data-evernote-id='197' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)controversyâ€‹  [Cliffs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/cliff) are an important topic. When they work well, cliffs are an effective and reasonably fair system to both employees and companies. But they can be abused and their complexity can lead to misunderstandings:

- â–ªThe intention of a [cliff](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/cliff) is to make sure new hires are committed to staying with the company for a significant period of time. However, the flip side of [vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting) with cliffs is that if an employee is leavingâ€”quits or is laid off or firedâ€”just short of their cliff, they may walk away with no stock ownership at all, sometimes through no fault of their own, as in the event of a family emergency or illness. In situations where companies fire or lay off employees just before a cliff, it can easily lead to hard feelings and even lawsuits (especially if the company is doing well enough that the stock is worth a lot of money).[*](https://www.inc.com/business-insider/tanium-security-startup-orion-hindawi-fired-employees-before-stocks-vested.html)[*](https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2017-09-19/tesla-worker-says-timing-of-firing-denied-him-lucrative-shares)
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='plain-diamond-outline' data-evernote-id='198' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)importantâ€‹ As a manager or founder, if an employee is performing poorly or may have to be laid off, itâ€™s both thoughtful and wise to let them know whatâ€™s going on well before their [cliff](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/cliff).
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-sigma' data-evernote-id='199' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)technicalâ€‹ Founders often have [vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting) on their stock themselves. As entrepreneur Dan Shapiro explains, this is often for [good reason](http://www.danshapiro.com/blog/2012/04/vesting-is-a-hack/).
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='plain-diamond-outline' data-evernote-id='200' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)importantâ€‹ As an employee, if youâ€™re leaving or considering leaving a company before your [vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting) cliff is met, consider waiting. Or, if your value to the company is high enough, you might [negotiate](https://www.businessinsider.com/everything-you-need-to-know-about-cliff-vesting-2011-5) to get some of your stock â€œ[vested up](https://www.foley.com/when-should-vesting-of-equity-grants-accelerate-06-19-2014/)â€ early. Your manager may well agree that is is fair for someone who has added a lot of value to the company to own stock even if they leave earlier than expected, especially for something like a family emergency. These kinds of vesting accelerations are entirely discretionary, however, unless you negotiated for special [acceleration](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/accelerated-vesting-and-single-trigger-and-double-trigger) in an employment agreement. Such special acceleration rights are typically reserved for executives who negotiate their employment offers heavily.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

- â–ª[Acceleration](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/accelerated-vesting-and-single-trigger-and-double-trigger) when a company is sold (called [*change of control*](http://stockoptioncounsel.com/blog/change-of-control-terms-for-startup-stock-options-restricted-stock-and-rsus/2018/6/4) terms) is common for founders and not so common for employees. Itâ€™s worth understanding acceleration and triggers in case they show up in your option agreement, but these may not be something you can negotiate unless you are going to be in a key role.
- â–ªCompanies may impose additional restrictions on stock that is vested. For example, your shares are very likely subject to a [right of first refusal](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/right-of-first-refusal-or-rofr), which means that you canâ€™t sell the stock without offering it first to the company. And it can happen that companies reserve the right to [repurchase](https://www.forbes.com/sites/dianahembree/2018/01/10/startup-employee-alert-can-your-company-take-back-your-vested-stock-options/#75fb48ee6e49) vested shares in certain events.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

### How Options Expire

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ The **exercise window (or exercise period)** is the period during which a person can buy shares at the [strike price](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/strike-price-or-exercise-price). Options are only exercisable for a fixed period of time, until they expire, typically seven to ten years as long as the person is working for the company. But this window is not always open.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-triangle' data-evernote-id='201' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)dangerâ€‹  **Expiration after termination.** Options can expire after you quit working for the company. Often, the expiration is **90 days** after termination of service, making the options effectively worthless if you cannot [exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise) before that point. As weâ€™ll get into later, you need to understand the costs, [taxesÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/taxes-on-isos-and-nsos), and tax [liabilitiesÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/the-amt-trap) of exercise and to plan ahead. In fact, you can find out when you are granted the options, or better yet, before you sign an [offer letter](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/verbal-offer-and-written-offer-and-offer-letter).

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='plain-diamond-outline' data-evernote-id='202' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)importantâ€‹  **Longer exercise windows.** Recently (since around 2015) a few companies are finding ways to keep the exercise window open for years after leaving a company, promoting this practice as fairer to employees. Companies with [extended exercise windows](https://github.com/holman/extended-exercise-windows) include Amplitude,[*](https://amplitude.com/blog/2015/12/01/employee-equity-is-broken-heres-our-fix/) Clef,[*](https://github.com/clef/handbook/blob/master/Hiring%20Documents/Guide%20to%20Your%20Equity.md) Coinbase,[*](https://medium.com/@barmstrong/improving-equity-compensation-at-coinbase-8749979409c3) Pinterest,[*](http://fortune.com/2015/03/23/pinterest-employee-taxes/) and Quora.[*](https://www.quora.com/Why-do-most-startups-force-employees-to-exercise-their-vested-ISO-options-within-90-days-if-they-leave-rather-than-the-option-to-convert-to-NSOs) However, the 90-day exercise window remains the norm.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-natural-disaster-hurricane' data-evernote-id='203' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)controversyâ€‹  **The exercise window debate.** Whether to have extended exercise windows has been debated at significant length. Some believe extended exercise windows are [the future](https://triplebyte.com/blog/extending-stock-option-exercise-window-guide#.12rv7ovrv), arguing that a shorter window makes a companyâ€™s success a [punishment](http://stockoptioncounsel.com/blog/nc7go8ivzxb1el5rhv6nltrjan0n2t/2017/3/6) to early employees.

Key considerations include:

- â–ªEveryone agrees that employees holding [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) with an expiring window often have to make a painful choice if they wish to leave: Pay for a substantial tax bill (perhaps five to seven figures) on top of the cost to [exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise) (possibly looking for [secondary liquidity or a loanÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/can-you-sell-private-stock)) or walk away from the options.
- â–ªMany familiar with this situation have spoken out [forcefully](https://zachholman.com/posts/fuck-your-90-day-exercise-window/) against shorter [exercise windows](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise-window-or-exercise-period), arguing that an employee can help grow the value of a company substantiallyâ€”often having taken a lower salary in exchange for equityâ€”but end up with [no ownership](https://triplebyte.com/blog/fixing-the-inequity-of-startup-equity) because theyâ€™re unable or unwilling to stay for the several years [typically neededÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/ipos) before an [IPO](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/initial-public-offering-or-ipo-and-goes-public) or sale.
- â–ªOn the other side, a few companies and investors [stand by](https://a16z.com/2016/06/23/options-timing/) the existing system, arguing that it is better to incentivize people not to leave a company, or that long windows effectively transfer wealth from employees who commit long-term to those who leave.
- â–ªSome focused on the legalities also argue that itâ€™s a legal requirement of [ISOs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/incentive-and-non-qualifying-and-statutory-and-non-statutory-stock-options) to have a 90-day [exercise window](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise-window-or-exercise-period). While this is technically true, itâ€™s not the [whole story](https://news.ycombinator.com/item?id=9254299). It is possible for companies to extend the exercise window by changing the nature of the options (converting them from ISOs to NSOs) and [many companies](https://github.com/holman/extended-exercise-windows) now choose to do just that.
- â–ªAnother path is to [split the difference](http://stockoptioncounsel.com/blog/early-expiration-of-startup-stock-options-part-2-the-full-10-year-term-solution/2017/3/28) and give extended windows only to longer-term employees.
- â–ªTaken together, itâ€™s evident many employees have not been clear on the nuances of this when joining companies, and some have â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-video-game-key' data-evernote-id='204' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)loginâ€‹[suffered](https://medium.com/@ben_mathes/90-days-and-my-six-figure-mistake-a495f4a188e2) because of it. With the risks of short [exercise windows](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise-window-or-exercise-period) for employees becoming more widely known, longer exercise windows are gradually becoming more prevalent. As an employee or a founder, it is fairer and wiser to understand and negotiate these things up front, and avoid unfortunate surprises.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='205' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ Options granted to advisors typically vest over a shorter period than employee grants, often one to two years. Advisor grants also typically have a longer [exercise window](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise-window-or-exercise-period) post termination of service, and will usually have [single trigger](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/accelerated-vesting-and-single-trigger-and-double-trigger) acceleration on an [acquisition](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/acquisition-and-sale), because no one expects advisors to stay on with a company once itâ€™s acquired. Typical terms for advisors, including equity levels, are available in the â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-download-thick-box' data-evernote-id='206' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cg stroke-linecap='round' fill='none' stroke-linejoin='round' stroke='currentColor' data-evernote-id='1564' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cpath d='M12%2c10.5v9' data-evernote-id='1565' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M15.001%2c16.5l-3%2c3l-3%2c-3' data-evernote-id='1566' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M2.251%2c23.25c-0.82842%2c0 -1.5%2c-0.67158 -1.5%2c-1.5v-13.5c0%2c-0.82842 0.67158%2c-1.5 1.5%2c-1.5h19.5c0.82842%2c0 1.5%2c0.67158 1.5%2c1.5v13.5c0%2c0.82842 -0.67158%2c1.5 -1.5%2c1.5Z' data-evernote-id='1567' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M0.954%2c7.5l2.4%2c-5.859l-5.46537e-08%2c1.23021e-07c0.240878%2c-0.542199 0.778703%2c-0.891471 1.372%2c-0.891h14.55l5.62502e-08%2c-7.13829e-12c0.592935%2c-7.5233e-05 1.13027%2c0.349133 1.371%2c0.891l2.344%2c5.765' data-evernote-id='1568' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M12%2c6.75v-6' data-evernote-id='1569' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3c/g%3e%3c/svg%3e)documentâ€‹[Founder/Advisor Standard Template (FAST)](https://fi.co/contents/fast#), from the Founder Institute.

### Kinds of Stock Options

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ Compensatory [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) come in two flavors, **incentive stock options (ISOs)** and **non-qualifying stock options (NQOs, or NQSOs)**. Confusingly, lawyers and the IRS use [several names](https://www.irs.gov/taxtopics/tc427) for these two kinds of stock options, including **statutory stock options** and **non-statutory stock options (or NSOs)**, respectively.

In this Guide, we refer to ISOs and NSOs.

| Type | Also called |
| --- | --- |
| Statutory | Incentive stock option, ISO |
| Non-statutory | Non-qualifying stock option, NQO, NQSO, NSO |

- â–ªCompanies generally decide to give ISOs or NSOs depending on the legal advice they get. Itâ€™s rarely up to the employee which they will receive, so itâ€™s best to know about both. There are pros and cons of each from both the recipientâ€™s and the companyâ€™s perspective.
- â–ªISOs are common for employees because they have the possibility of being more favorable from a tax point of view than NSOs.
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-diamond' data-evernote-id='207' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)cautionâ€‹  [ISOs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/incentive-and-non-qualifying-and-statutory-and-non-statutory-stock-options) can only be granted to employees (not independent contractors or directors who are not also employees).
- â–ªBut [ISOs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/incentive-and-non-qualifying-and-statutory-and-non-statutory-stock-options) have a number of limitations and conditions and can also create difficult [tax consequencesÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/taxes-on-isos-and-nsos).

### Early Exercise

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ Sometimes, to help reduce the tax burden on [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), a company will make it possible for option holders to **early exercise (or forward exercise)** their options, which means they can [exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise) even before they vest. The option holder becomes a [stockholder](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) sooner, after which the [vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting) applies to actual stock rather than options. This will have [tax implicationsÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/83b-elections).

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-diamond' data-evernote-id='208' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)cautionâ€‹ However, the company has the right to repurchase the *unvested* shares, at the price paid or at the [fair market value](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/fair-market-value-or-fmv) of the shares (whichever is lower), if a person quits working for the company. The company will typically repurchase the unvested shares should the person leave the company before the stock theyâ€™ve purchased vests.

Get all of our latest posts in one weekly email. Delivered on Wednesdays.

Emails may include promotional content from Holloway. You may opt out at any time.

### Restricted Stock Units

While [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) are the most common form of equity compensation in smaller [private companies](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies), [RSUs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-units-or-rsus) have become the most common type of equity award for public and large private companies. Facebook pioneered the use of RSUs as a private company to allow it to avoid having to register as a [public company](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies) earlier.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  [**Restricted stock units (RSUs)**](http://www.investopedia.com/terms/r/restricted-stock-unit.asp) refer to an agreement by a company to issue an employee shares of stock or the cash value of shares of stock on a future date. Each unit represents one share of stock or the cash value of one share of stock that the employee will receive in the future. (Theyâ€™re called *units* since they are neither stock nor [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), but another thing altogether that is contractually linked to the value of stock.)

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ The date on which an employee receives the shares or cash payment for RSUs is known as the **settlement date**.

- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-diamond' data-evernote-id='209' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)cautionâ€‹ RSUs may vest according to a [vesting schedule](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting-schedule). The settlement date may be the time-based [vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting) date or a later date based on, for instance, the date of a companyâ€™s [IPO](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/initial-public-offering-or-ipo-and-goes-public).
- â–ªRSUs are difficult in a startup or early stage company because when the RSUs vest, the value of the shares might be significant, and taxes will be owed on the receipt of the shares.[*](http://thestartuplawblog.com/rsus-the-tax-problems/) This is not a bad result when the company has sufficient capital to help the employee make the tax payments, or the company is a [public company](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/public-companies) that has put in place a program for selling shares to pay the taxes. But for cash-strapped private startups, neither of these are possibilities. This is the reason most startups use [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) rather than RSUs or [stock awards](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants).
- â–ª[RSUs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-units-or-rsus) are often considered less preferable to grantees since they remove control over when you owe tax. Options, if granted with an [exercise price](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/strike-price-or-exercise-price) equal to the [fair market value](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/fair-market-value-or-fmv) of the stock, are not taxed until [exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise), an event under the control of the optionee. If an employee is awarded an RSU or [restricted stock award](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants) which vests over time, they will be taxed on the [vesting schedule](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting-schedule); they have been put on â€œautopilotâ€ with respect to the timing of the tax event. If the shares are worth a lot on the date of [vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting), the tax burden can be significant.
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='210' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ You donâ€™t want to confuse *[restricted stock units](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-units-or-rsus)* with *[restricted stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants)*, which typically refers to [restricted stock awards](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants).

### Less Common Types of Equity

While most employee equity compensation takes the form of stock, [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), or [RSUs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-units-or-rsus), a complete tour of equity compensation must mention a few less common forms.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Phantom equity** is a type of compensation award that references equity, but does not entitle the recipient to actual ownership in a company. These awards come under a variety of different monikers, but the key to understanding them is knowing that they are really just cash bonus plans, where the cash amounts are determined by reference to a companyâ€™s stock. Phantom equity can have significant value, but may be perceived as less valuable by workers because of the contractual nature of the promises. Phantom equity plans can be set up as purely discretionary bonus plans, which is less attractive than owning a piece of something.

Two examples of phantom equity are phantom stock and [stock appreciation rights](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-appreciation-rights-or-sars):

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ A [**phantom stock**](http://www.investopedia.com/articles/stocks/12/introduction-phantom-stock.asp) award is a type of phantom equity that entitles the recipient to a payment equal to the value of a share of the companyâ€™s stock, upon the occurrence of certain events.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  [**Stock appreciation rights (SARs)**](https://www.nceo.org/articles/phantom-stock-appreciation-rights-sars) are a type of phantom equity that gives the recipient the right to receive a payment calculated by reference to the appreciation in the equity of the company.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  [**Warrants**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warrant_%28finance%29) are another kind of option to purchase stock, generally used in investment transactions (for example, in a convertible note offering, investors may also get a warrant, or a law firm may ask for one in exchange for vendor financing). They differ from [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) in that they are more abbreviated and stand-alone legal documents, not granted pursuant to a single legal agreement (typically called a â€œ[planÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/documents-and-agreements)â€) for all employees.

Employees and advisors may not encounter warrants, but itâ€™s worth knowing they exist.

## [Tax Basics](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/tax-basics)11 minutes, 45 links

The awarding of equity compensation can give rise to multiple types of taxes for the recipient, including federal and state [income taxes](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/income-tax) and [employment taxes](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/employment-taxes-and-payroll-taxes). Thereâ€™s a lot that you have to be aware of. [Skip aheadÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/taxes-on-equity-compensation) to understand how taxes on equity work, but if you have time, this section gives a technical summary of tax fundamentals, just in case you never really figured out all the numbers on your pay stub.

You donâ€™t need to know every detail, and can rely on software and professionals to determine the tax you owe, but we do suggest understanding the different kinds of taxes, how large they can be, and how each is â€œtriggeredâ€ by different events.

Given the complexity, most taxpayers arenâ€™t aware of exactly how their tax is calculated. It does take up thousands of pages[*](http://www.slate.com/articles/news_and_politics/politics/2014/04/how_long_is_the_tax_code_it_is_far_shorter_than_70_000_pages.html) of the federal tax code and involves the intricate diversity of state tax law as well.[*](https://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/USCODE-2016-title26/content-detail.html)

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='211' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ If youâ€™re already familiar with tax terminology, this section may not have any major surprises. But for those who are not used to it, watch out: Many terms sound like regular English, but theyâ€™re not. *[Ordinary income](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/income-and-ordinary-income-and-capital-gains)*, *long-term* and *short-term*, *election*, *qualified small business*, and other phrases have very specific meanings weâ€™ll do our best to spell out.

### Kinds of Income

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Income** is the money an individual makes. For tax purposes, there are two main types of income, which are taxed differently. [**Ordinary income**](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/o/ordinaryincome.asp) includes wages, salary, bonuses and interest made on investments. [**Capital gains**](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/c/capital_gains_tax.asp) are the profits an individual makes from selling assets, including stock.

One key difference between ordinary income and capital gains is that when capital gains taxes are calculated, consideration is given not just to the sale price of the asset but to the total gain or loss the investment incurred, each outcome having significantly different tax consequences.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹ Capital gains are classified as long-term or short-term. **Long-term capital gains** are the profits an individual makes from selling assets, such as stock, a business, a house, or land, that were held for more than a year. **Short-term capital gains** are profits from the sale of assets held for less than a year.

Although this topic is not without â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-money-bag-dollar' data-evernote-id='212' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)paidâ€‹[controversy](https://www.wsj.com/articles/how-should-capital-gains-be-taxed-1425271052), the general idea is, if you are selling something youâ€™ve owned for a long time, you can be taxed a lower rate.

All these rates have evolved over time based on economic and political factors,[*](https://www.taxpolicycenter.org/briefing-book/how-are-capital-gains-taxed) so you can be confident they will change again in the future.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-new-circle' data-evernote-id='213' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cg stroke-linecap='round' stroke-width='1' stroke='currentColor' fill='none' stroke-linejoin='round' data-evernote-id='1504' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3cpath d='M5.499%2c15.5v-6l3%2c6v-6' data-evernote-id='1505' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M13.52%2c15.5h-2l-4.37114e-08%2c-1.77636e-15c-0.552285%2c-2.41411e-08 -1%2c-0.447715 -1%2c-1c0%2c0 0%2c0 0%2c0v-4l1.06581e-14%2c1.50996e-07c-8.33927e-08%2c-0.552285 0.447715%2c-1 1%2c-1h2' data-evernote-id='1506' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M10.52%2c12.5h2' data-evernote-id='1507' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M19.5%2c9.5v1.764l2.46157e-07%2c-0.00216047c0%2c1.47044 -0.342023%2c2.92073 -0.999035%2c4.23623l-1%2c-4l-1%2c4l8.62533e-08%2c1.72462e-07c-0.657762%2c-1.31518 -1.00014%2c-2.7655 -1%2c-4.236v-1.76207' data-evernote-id='1508' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3cpath d='M20.1317%2c3.86827c4.49103%2c4.49103 4.49103%2c11.7724 0%2c16.2635c-4.49103%2c4.49103 -11.7724%2c4.49103 -16.2635%2c0c-4.49103%2c-4.49103 -4.49103%2c-11.7724 0%2c-16.2635c4.49103%2c-4.49103 11.7724%2c-4.49103 16.2635%2c0' data-evernote-id='1509' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/path%3e%3c/g%3e%3c/svg%3e)newâ€‹ In 2017, Congress passed the [Tax Cuts and Jobs Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tax_Cuts_and_Jobs_Act_of_2017) (TCJA), which made [many changes](https://heritageinvestment.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/01/TCJA-HIG-Old-vs.-New-Comparison.pdf) to tax rates for the **2018** tax year. [Long-term capital gains taxes](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/long-term-capital-gains-tax) did [not change](https://www.marketwatch.com/story/your-simple-guide-to-the-new-capital-gains-tax-rates-2018-04-16) significantly.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

### Federal Taxes

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Income tax** is the money paid by individuals to federal, state, and, in some cases, local governments, and includes taxation of [ordinary income](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/income-and-ordinary-income-and-capital-gains) and [capital gains](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/income-and-ordinary-income-and-capital-gains). Generally, U.S. citizens, residents, and some [foreigners](https://www.irs.gov/individuals/international-taxpayers/nra-withholding) must [file](https://www.irs.gov/pub/irs-pdf/p17.pdf) and pay federal [income](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/income-and-ordinary-income-and-capital-gains) tax.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='plain-diamond-outline' data-evernote-id='214' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)importantâ€‹ In general, federal tax applies to many [kinds of income](https://www.irs.gov/taxtopics/tc400.html). If youâ€™re an employee at a startup, you need to consider four kinds of federal tax, each of which is computed differently.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-task-finger-bandage' data-evernote-id='215' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)confusionâ€‹ When it comes to equity compensation, itâ€™s possible that youâ€™ll have to worry about *all of these*, depending on your situation. Thatâ€™s why we have a lot to cover here:

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Ordinary income tax** is the tax on wages or salary [income](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/income-and-ordinary-income-and-capital-gains), and short-term investment income. The term **short-term capital gains tax** may be applied to taxes on assets sold less than a year from purchase, but profits from these sales are taxed as [ordinary income](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/income-and-ordinary-income-and-capital-gains). For a lot of people who make most of their money by working, ordinary income tax is the biggest chunk of tax they pay.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Employment taxes** are an additional kind of federal tax beyond ordinary income tax, and consist of Social Security and [Medicare taxes](https://www.irs.gov/businesses/small-businesses-self-employed/questions-and-answers-for-the-additional-medicare-tax) that are withheld from a personâ€™s paycheck. Employment taxes are also referred to as [**payroll taxes**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Payroll_tax) as they often show up on employee pay stubs. The Social Security wage withholding rate in 2018 is 6.2% up to the FICA wage base. The Medicare component is 1.45%, and it does not phase out above the FICA wage base.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Long-term capital gains tax** is a tax on the sale of assets held longer than a year. [Long-term capital gains](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/long-term-capital-gains-and-short-term-capital-gains) tax is often lower than ordinary income tax. Many investors hold assets for longer than a year in order to qualify for the lesser tax burden of long-term capital gains.

â€‹Definitionâ€‹  **Alternative minimum tax (AMT)** is a [supplemental income tax](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alternative_minimum_tax) that applies to certain individuals in some situations. This type of tax does not come up for many taxpayers, but higher [income](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/income-and-ordinary-income-and-capital-gains) earners and people in special situations may have to pay large AMT bills. AMT was first enacted in 1979 in response to reports that 155 wealthy individuals had paid no [income tax](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/income-tax) in 1966.[*](https://www.taxpolicycenter.org/briefing-book/what-amt) It is not the same as [ordinary income tax](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/ordinary-income-tax-and-short-term-capital-gains-tax) or employment tax, and is calculated according to its [own rules](https://www.nerdwallet.com/blog/taxes/alternative-minimum-tax-amt/).

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-triangle' data-evernote-id='216' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)dangerâ€‹ AMT is relevant to you if youâ€™re reading this. Itâ€™s important to understand because exercising [ISOs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/incentive-and-non-qualifying-and-statutory-and-non-statutory-stock-options) can trigger AMT. In some cases a *lot* of AMT, *even when you havenâ€™t sold the stock* and have no money to pay. We discuss this [laterÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/the-amt-trap).

â€‹
