### Questions Candidates Can Ask

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='plain-diamond-outline' data-evernote-id='258' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)importantâ€‹ Itâ€™s important to ask questions when you get an offer that includes any kind of equity. In addition to helping you learn the facts about the equity offer, the process of discussing these details can help you get a sense of the companyâ€™s transparency and responsiveness. Here are a few questions you should consider asking, especially if youâ€™re evaluating an offer from a startup or another [private company](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/private-companies):

- â–ª

**[Percentage ownership](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/percentage-ownership-and-basis-points).**

    - â–«*What percentage of the company do the shares represent?*
    - â–«*What set of shares was used to compute that percentage? Is it [outstanding shares](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/outstanding-shares) or [fully diluted](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/fully-diluted)?*
    - â–«*What convertible securities are outstanding (convertible notes, SAFEs, or warrants), and how much [dilution](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/dilution) can I expect from their conversion?*
- â–ª

**[Valuation](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/valuation).**

    - â–«*What did the last round value the company at? (That is, what is the preferred share price times the total [outstanding shares](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/outstanding-shares)?)*
    - â–«*What is the most recent [409A valuation](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/409a-valuation)? When was it done, and will it be done again soon?*
    - â–«*What [exit](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/liquidity-and-exits-and-liquidity-events-and-liquidations) valuation will need to be achieved before [common stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/common-stock-and-preferred-stock) has positive value (that is, what are the [liquidation](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/liquidity-and-exits-and-liquidity-events-and-liquidations) overhangs)?*
- â–ª

**[Stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options).**

    - â–«*Do you allow [early exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/early-exercise-or-forward-exercise) of my options?*
    - â–«*Am I required to [exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise) my options within 90 days after I leave or am terminated? Does the company extend the [exercise window](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise-window-or-exercise-period) of the options of employees that depart?*
- â–ª

**[Vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting).**

    - â–«*Are all employees on the same [vesting schedule](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting-schedule)?*
    - â–«*Is there any [acceleration](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/accelerated-vesting-and-single-trigger-and-double-trigger) of my vesting if the company is acquired?*
    - â–«*Do you have a policy regarding follow-on [stock grants](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants)?*
    - â–«*Does the company have any repurchase right to vested shares?*

This information will help you consider the benefits and drawbacks of possible [exercise scenariosÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/stock-option-scenarios).

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='plain-diamond-outline' data-evernote-id='259' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)importantâ€‹ If youâ€™re considering working for a startup, there are further questions to ask in order to assess the state of the companyâ€™s business and its plans. Before or when youâ€™re getting an offer is the right time to do this. Startups are understandably careful about sharing financial information, so you may not get full answers to all of these, but you should at least ask:

- â–ª*How much money has the company raised (including in how many rounds, and when)?*
- â–ª*What did the last round value the company at?*
- â–ª*What is the aggregate [liquidation preference](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/liquidation-preference-or-preference) on top of the [preferred stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/common-stock-and-preferred-stock)?* (This will tell you how much the company needs to sell for before the common stockâ€”your equityâ€”is worth something in an [exit](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/liquidity-and-exits-and-liquidity-events-and-liquidations).)
- â–ª*Will the company likely raise more capital soon?*
- â–ª*How long will the companyâ€™s current funding last?* (This will likely be given at the current burn rate, or how quickly a company is spending its funding, so will likely not include calculations for things like future employee salaries.)
- â–ª*What is the hiring plan? (How many people over what time frame?)*
- â–ª*What is the revenue now, if any? What are the revenue goals/projections?*
- â–ª*Where do you see this company in 1 year and 5 years, in terms of revenue, number of employees, and market position?*

There are several other [resources](https://blog.wealthfront.com/stock-options-14-crucial-questions/) with [more questions](http://www.inc.com/atish-davda/5-questions-you-should-ask-before-taking-a-start-up-job-offer.html) like this to consider.

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

### Typical Employee Equity Levels

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

Compensation data is highly situational. What an employee receives in equity, cash, and benefits depends on the role theyâ€™re filling, the sector they work in, where they and the company are located, and the possible value that specific individual may bring to the company.

Any compensation data out there is hard to come by. Companies often pay for this data from [vendors](https://www.advanced-hr.com/products-overview), but itâ€™s usually not available to candidates.

For startups, a variety of data is easier to come by. We give some overview here of early-stage Silicon Valley tech startups; many of these numbers are not representative of companies of different kinds across the country:

- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='plain-diamond-outline' data-evernote-id='260' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)importantâ€‹ One of the best ways to tell what is reasonable for a given company and candidate is to look at offers from companies with similar profiles on [AngelList](https://angel.co/). The [**AngelList salary data**](https://angel.co/salaries) is extensive.
- â–ª

There are no hard and fast rules, but for **post-series A startups** in **Silicon Valley**, the table below, based on [the one by Babak Nivi](http://venturehacks.com/articles/option-pool-shuffle#market), gives ballpark equity levels that many think are reasonable. These would usually be for [restricted stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants) or [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) with a standard 4-year [vesting schedule](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting-schedule). They apply if each of these roles were filled just after an A round and the new hires are also being paid a salary (so are not founders or employees hired before the A round). The upper ranges would be for highly desired candidates with strong track records.

    - â–«Chief executive officer (CEO): **5â€“10%**
    - â–«Chief operating officer (COO): **2â€“5%**
    - â–«Vice president (VP): **1â€“2%**
    - â–«Independent [board member](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/board-of-directors-and-inside-directors-and-outside-directors-and-board-members-and-board-seat): **1%**
    - â–«Director: **0.4â€“1.25%**
    - â–«Lead engineer **0.5â€“1%**
    - â–«Senior engineer: **0.33â€“0.66%**
    - â–«Manager or junior engineer: **0.2â€“0.33%**
- â–ªFor **post-series B startups**, equity numbers would be much lower. How much lower will depend significantly on the size of the team and the companyâ€™s [valuation](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/valuation).
- â–ªSeed-funded startups would offer higher equityâ€”sometimes much higher if there is little funding, but base salaries will be lower.
- â–ª

Leo Polovets created a [survey](http://codingvc.com/analyzing-angellist-job-postings-part-2-salary-and-equity-benchmarks) of AngelList job postings from 2014, an excellent summary of equity levels for the first few dozen hires at these early-stage startups. For **engineers** in Silicon Valley, the highest (not typical!) equity levels were:

    - â–«Hire #1: up to **2%â€“3%**
    - â–«Hires #2 through #5: up to **1%â€“2%**
    - â–«Hires #6 and #7: up to **0.5%â€“1%**
    - â–«Hires #8 through #14: up to **0.4%â€“0.8%**
    - â–«Hires #15 through #19: up to **0.3%â€“0.7%**
    - â–«Hires #21 [sic] through #27: up to **0.25%â€“0.6%**
    - â–«Hires #28 through #34: up to **0.25%â€“0.5%**
- â–ªJosÃ© Ancer gives another good [overview](http://siliconhillslawyer.com/2018/05/08/early-startup-employee-compensation/) for early stage hiring.
- â–ª*Founder* compensation is another topic entirely that may still be of interest to employees. JosÃ© Ancer provides a thoughtful [overview](http://siliconhillslawyer.com/2016/06/23/founder-compensation-cash-equity-liquidity/).

### Negotiation Tips

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹

When negotiating a job offer, companies will always ask you what you want for compensation, and you should always be [cautious](http://review.chicagobooth.edu/strategy/2018/article/how-answer-one-toughest-interview-questions) about answering.

If you name the lowest number youâ€™ll accept, you can be pretty sure the companyâ€™s not going to exceed it, at least not by much.

â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-diamond' data-evernote-id='261' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)cautionâ€‹ Asking about salary expectations is a normal part of the hiring process at most companies, but asking about **salary history** has been banned in a growing number of states, cities, and counties.[*](https://www.hrdive.com/news/salary-history-ban-states-list/516662/) These laws attempt to combat pay disparity[*](https://www.nytimes.com/2018/02/16/business/economy/salary-history-laws.html) among women and minorities by making it illegal for companies to ask about or consider candidatesâ€™ current or past compensation when making them offers. Make sure you understand the laws relevant to your situation.

A few points on negotiating compensation:

- â–ªSome argue that a [good tactic](http://www.businessinsider.com/how-to-negotiate-make-first-offer-2014-5) in negotiating is to start higher than you will be willing to accept, so that the other party can â€œwinâ€ by negotiating you down a little bit. Keep in mind, this is just a suggested tactic, not a hard and fast rule.
- â–ªIf you are inexperienced and unsure what a fair offer should look like, avoid saying exactly what you want for compensation very early in discussions. Though many hiring managers and recruiters ask about [salary expectations](http://fistfuloftalent.com/2018/01/ask-salary-without-asking-salary-expectations.html) early in the process to avoid risk at the offer stage, some ask in order to take advantage of candidates who donâ€™t have a good sense of [their own worth](https://nuleadership.com/2018/01/08/know-your-worth-compensation-negotiation/). Tell them you want to focus on the opportunity as a whole and your ability to contribute before discussing numbers. Ask them to give you a fair offer once they understand what you can bring to the company.
- â–ªIf you are experienced and know your value, itâ€™s often in your interest to state what sort of compensation and role you are looking for to anchor expectations. You might even share your expectations early in the process, so you donâ€™t waste each otherâ€™s time.
- â–ªDiscuss what your compensation might be like in the future. No one can promise you future equity, salary, or bonuses, but it should be possible to agree what those could look like *if* you demonstrate outstanding performance and the company has money.
- â–ªIf youâ€™re moving from an established company to a startup, you may be asked to take a [salary cut](https://www.quora.com/Should-you-take-a-salary-cut-if-you-join-a-startup). This is reasonable, but itâ€™s wise to discuss explicitly how much the cut is, and when your salary will be renegotiated. For example, you might take 25% below your previous salary, but there can be an agreement that this will be corrected if your performance is strong and the company gets funding.
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='plain-diamond-outline' data-evernote-id='262' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)importantâ€‹ Always negotiate [non-compensation](http://www.kennedyexecutive.com/blog/salary-negotiation-33-things-negotiate-money/) aspects before agreeing to an offer. If you want a specific role, title, opportunity, visa sponsorship, parental leave, special treatment (like working from home), or have timing constraints about when you can join, [negotiate these early](https://www.nerdwallet.com/blog/loans/student-loans/negotiate-nonsalary-benefits-job-perks/), not late in the process.
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='plain-diamond-outline' data-evernote-id='263' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)importantâ€‹ If youâ€™re going to be a very early employee, consider asking for a [restricted stock](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants) grant instead of [stock options](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options), and a cash bonus equal to the tax on those options. The company will have some extra paperwork (and legal costs), but it means you wonâ€™t have to pay to [exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise). Then, if you file an [83(b) election](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/83-b-election), youâ€™re simplifying your situation even further, eliminating the [AMT issuesÂ§](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/the-amt-trap) of [ISOs](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/incentive-and-non-qualifying-and-statutory-and-non-statutory-stock-options), and maximizing your chances of qualifying for [long-term capital gains tax](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/long-term-capital-gains-tax).

â€‹Â·Â·Â·â€‹
A few notes on the negotiation process itself:

- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='plain-diamond-outline' data-evernote-id='264' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)importantâ€‹ Although [offer letters](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/verbal-offer-and-written-offer-and-offer-letter) have expirations, itâ€™s often possible to negotiate more time if you need it. How much flexibility depends on the situation. Some have criticized â€œexploding job offersâ€ as a [bad practice](https://www.huffingtonpost.com/adam-grant/its-time-to-eliminate-exp_b_4594222.html) that makes [no sense](https://erikbern.com/2016/03/16/exploding-offers-are-bullshit.html) at all. If you are likely the best candidate for the position, or the role is a specialized and well-paid one where there are usually not enough good candidates to meet the demand, youâ€™ll likely have plenty of leverage to [ask for more time](http://www.businessinsider.com/how-to-politely-postpone-accepting-a-job-offer-2015-6#dont-be-afraid-to-negotiate-4), which may be needed to complete the interview process with other companies. Software engineering roles in tech companies are like this currently.
- â–ª

Getting [multiple offers](https://www.themuse.com/advice/a-guide-to-juggling-multiple-job-offers-and-coming-out-on-top) is always in your interest. If you have competing offers, sharing the competing offers with the company you want to work for can be helpful, granted your offers are competitive.

    - â–«However, dragging out negotiations excessively so you can â€œshop aroundâ€ an offer to other companies is considered bad form by some; itâ€™s thoughtful to be judicious and timely to the extent that itâ€™s possible.
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-triangle' data-evernote-id='265' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)dangerâ€‹ Get all agreements in writing, if they are not in your [offer letter](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/verbal-offer-and-written-offer-and-offer-letter).
- â–ªDo not accept an offer verbally or in writing unless youâ€™re ready to stand by your word. In practice, people do occasionally accept an offer and then go back on it, or [*renege*](https://purduecco.wordpress.com/2016/03/28/the-risks-of-reneging-on-a-job-offer/). This can put the company in a difficult position (they may have declined another key candidate based on your acceptance), and may hurt your reputation in unexpected ways later.

Some additional resources:

- â–ªHarvard Business Review has a variety of general â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-money-bag-dollar' data-evernote-id='266' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)paidâ€‹[suggestions](https://hbr.org/2014/04/15-rules-for-negotiating-a-job-offer) on negotiation processes.
- â–ªRobby Grossman, a VP at Wistia, gives a good [overview](http://rob.by/2013/negotiating-your-startup-job-offer) of equity compensation and negotiation suggestions in startups.

### Offer and Negotiation Dangers

To wind up our discussion of offers and negotiations, here are some key dangers and mistakes to watch out for:

- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-triangle' data-evernote-id='267' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)dangerâ€‹ Do not accept an offer of stock or shares without also asking for the exact number of total shares (or, equivalently, the exact percentage of the company those shares represent). Itâ€™s quite common for some companies to give offers of stock or options and tell you only the number of shares. Without the percentage, the number of shares is meaningless. Not telling you is a deeply unfair practice. A company that refuses to tell you even when youâ€™re ready to sign an offer is likely giving you a very poor deal.
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-diamond' data-evernote-id='268' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)cautionâ€‹ If youâ€™re looking at an offer, work out whether you can and should [early exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/early-exercise-or-forward-exercise), and what the cost to [exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise) and tax will be, before accepting the offer.
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-triangle' data-evernote-id='269' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)dangerâ€‹ If you join a startup right as it raises a new round, and donâ€™t have the chance to [exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise) right away, they may potentially issue you the options with the low [strike price](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/strike-price-or-exercise-price), but the [409A valuation](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/409a-valuation) of the stock will have gone up. This means you wonâ€™t be able to [early exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/early-exercise-or-forward-exercise) without a large tax bill. In fact, it might not be financially feasible for you to exercise at all.
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-triangle' data-evernote-id='270' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)dangerâ€‹  [Vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting) starts on a vesting commencement date. Sometimes [stock option](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) paperwork wonâ€™t reach you for weeks or months after you join a company, since it needs to be written by the lawyers and approved by the [board of directors](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/board-of-directors-and-inside-directors-and-outside-directors-and-board-members-and-board-seat). In your negotiations, do make sure the vesting commencement date will reflect the true start date of when you joined the company, not the time at which the stock option is granted.
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-diamond' data-evernote-id='271' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)cautionâ€‹ The [offer letter](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/verbal-offer-and-written-offer-and-offer-letter) is not the actual grant of your equity. After you sign your offer letter, ensure the company delivers you your actual equity grant documents within a few weeks. It is not uncommon for early-stage startups to be sloppy with their equity granting. If they take too long to send your grant documents, the [fair market value](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/fair-market-value-or-fmv) (and [exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise) price) of the equity could rise in the time youâ€™re waiting, which is money lost for you.
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-diamond' data-evernote-id='272' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)cautionâ€‹ If youâ€™re going to [early exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/early-exercise-or-forward-exercise), consider it like any investment. Donâ€™t believe every projection about the value of the company you hear. Founders will tell you the best-case scenario. Remember, most startups fail. Do your research and ask othersâ€™ opinions about likely outcomes for the company.
- â–ªâ€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-alert-triangle' data-evernote-id='273' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)dangerâ€‹ It may not be common, but some companies retain a right to repurchase (buy back) vested shares. Itâ€™s simple enough to ask, â€œDoes the company have any repurchase right to *vested* shares?â€ (Note repurchasing *unvested* shares that were purchased via [early exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/early-exercise-or-forward-exercise) is different, and helps you.) If you donâ€™t want to ask, the [fair market value](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/fair-market-value-or-fmv) repurchase right should be included in the documents you are being asked to sign or acknowledge that you have read and understood. (Skypeâ€™s [controversy](https://techcrunch.com/2011/06/26/skypes-worthless-employee-stock-option-plan-heres-why-they-did-it/) related to repurchasing has some startup employees [looking out](https://www.quora.com/Which-valley-startups-have-a-Skype-like-repurchase-right) for companies with similar plans.) You might find a repurchase right for vested shares in the Stock Plan itself, the [Stock Option](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) Agreement, the [Exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/exercise) Agreement, the bylaws, the certificate of [incorporation](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/incorporation-and-incorporating), or any other [stockholder](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-and-shares-and-shareholder-or-stockholder) agreement.

## [Documents and Agreements](https://www.holloway.com/g/equity-compensation/sections/documents-and-agreements)8 links

This section covers a few kinds of documents youâ€™re likely to see as you negotiate a job offer and sign on to a company. Itâ€™s not exhaustive, as titles and details vary.

- â–ª

When you are considering your offer, make sure you have all of the documents you need from the company:

    - â–«Your â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-download-thick-box' data-evernote-id='274' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)documentâ€‹[offer letter](https://www.upcounsel.com/employee-offer-letter), which will detail salary, benefits, and equity compensation.
    - â–«An â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-download-thick-box' data-evernote-id='275' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)documentâ€‹[Employee Innovations Agreement](https://recruit.smashfly.com/SmashFlyMedia/Docs/12250/12250_168_United%20States%20-%20EIPIA%20English%20%28Rev%2014%20FEBRUARY%202017%29%202%20pages.pdf), Proprietary Information and Inventions Assignment Agreement, or similar, concerning intellectual property.
- â–ª

If you have equity compensation, at some pointâ€”possibly weeks or months after youâ€™ve joinedâ€”you should get a Summary of [Stock Grant](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants), Notice of [Stock Option](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/stock-options) Grant, or similar document, detailing your grant of stock or options, along with all details such as number of shares, type of options, grant date, [vesting](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting) commencement date, and [vesting schedule](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/vesting-schedule). It will come with several other documents, which may be exhibits to that agreement:

    - â–«â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-download-thick-box' data-evernote-id='276' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)documentâ€‹[Stock Option Agreement](https://www.upcounsel.com/stock-option-agreement)
    - â–«â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-download-thick-box' data-evernote-id='277' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)documentâ€‹[Stock Plan](https://www.upcounsel.com/equity-incentive-plan) (sometimes called a Stock Option Plan, or [Stock Award](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/restricted-stock-award-and-restricted-stock-and-stock-awards-and-stock-grants) Plan, or Equity Incentive Plan)
    - â–«â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-download-thick-box' data-evernote-id='278' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)documentâ€‹[Code Section 409A Waiver and Release](https://www.lawinsider.com/clause/code-section-409a-waiver-and-release) (sometimes part of the Stock Option Agreement)
- â–ª

If you are exercising your options, you should also see paperwork to assist with that purchase:

    - â–«â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-download-thick-box' data-evernote-id='279' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)documentâ€‹[Exercise Agreement](https://www.upcounsel.com/option-exercise-agreement)
    - â–«Instructions and template for [early exercise](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/early-exercise-or-forward-exercise) and â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-download-thick-box' data-evernote-id='280' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)documentâ€‹[83(b) election](https://www.irs.gov/pub/irs-drop/rp-12-29.pdf), if applicable
- â–ª

End of year tax documents

    - â–«You should receive a form â€‹![](data:image/svg+xml,%3csvg xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg' aria-labelledby='str-download-thick-box' data-evernote-id='281' class='js-evernote-checked'%3e%3c/svg%3e)documentâ€‹[3921 or 3922](https://www.irs.gov/uac/form-3921-exercise-of-an-incentive-stock-option-under-section-422-b) from your company if you exercised [ISO](https://www.holloway.com/definitions/incentive-and-non-qualifying-and-statutory-and-non-statutory-stock-options) options during the year.
